He said: Whoever buys a slave and claims that he has run away, the seller is not made to swear until the buyer establishes evidence that he ran away in his possession. And the intended meaning is an oath that he did not run away at his (seller's) place. Because though the statement is his (the seller's), his denial is only considered after the defect is established in the buyer's hand and its knowledge by proof. Translation: He said: Whoever buys a slave and claims that he is a runaway, the seller will not be made to swear until the buyer establishes evidence that this slave ran away while with him. And the purpose of making the seller swear is to make him swear that the slave did not run away at his place. Because even though the statement (the seller's denial) is accepted, his denial is only valid after the defect is established in the possession of the buyer, and its knowledge is through evidence. Explanation: It has passed before that a slave's being a runaway is a defect, but due to it the buyer only has the right to return the slave when this defect also repeats in the same state at the buyer's place as it appeared at the seller's. So if the slave ran away from the seller's place but did not run away while in the buyer's possession, or if he ran away at the seller's before puberty and ran away at the buyer's after puberty, then in both cases the buyer does not have the right to return the slave. The scenario: after this preface, the situation is that the buyer claimed the slave is a runaway and the seller denied it. Then the judge will not hear the buyer's claim, but will tell the buyer: first prove that the slave ran away at your place. So if the buyer establishes evidence that the slave ran away at his place, then the judge will hear his claim and turn to the seller, saying: 'Was this defect of running away present at your place in the same state as it is at the buyer's or not?' If the seller says, 'Yes, this defect was present at my place in the same state as the buyer claims,' then the buyer will have the right to return the slave. And if the seller denies that the defect existed at his place or claims a difference in state, for example, the buyer claims the slave ran away after puberty and the seller says he ran away before puberty, then the judge will tell the buyer: 'Present evidence that this defect existed in the slave at the seller's place in the same state as you claim at your place.' If the buyer presents evidence on this, then the buyer has the right to return the slave under the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb). And if the buyer cannot present evidence and requests an oath from the seller that there is no defect, then the seller is made to swear that this slave never ran away at my place. The seller will not be made to swear until the buyer establishes evidence of the defect at his own place. Because even though the seller's denial is accepted due to him being the denier, his denial is only valid after the defect is first established in the buyer's possession. And the proof of the defect in the buyer's possession is through witnesses. Therefore, the buyer should first present evidence of the defect at his place, then the seller is made to swear. When the buyer has established the evidence, what words will the seller be sworn with? So if he establishes it, he swears by Allah Almighty: 'I sold it and delivered it to him, and it never ran away at my place.' Thus it is said in the book. And if he wishes, he may make him swear: 'By Allah, you have no right to return it to me on the basis which you claim,' or 'By Allah, it never ran away at your place.' But he may not make him swear: 'By Allah, I sold it and it had no such defect,' nor 'By Allah, I sold it and delivered it and it had no such defect,' because that would be neglecting consideration for the buyer, for a defect may occur after the sale before delivery, which would necessitate return. The first (formulation) neglects this, and the second suggests it is linked to both conditions, so it is taken in the oath at the time of its occurrence at delivery, not at the time of sale.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi procedure when a buyer claims that a purchased slave is a habitual runaway (abaaq). The ruling is that the seller cannot be asked to swear an oath about the defect at his end until the buyer first proves that the slave actually ran away while in the buyer's possession. This is because the seller's denial is only meaningful after the defect is established in the buyer's hand. The commentary provides a detailed breakdown: the buyer must first establish with evidence that the slave ran away during his own ownership. Only then does the judge turn to the seller to ask whether the same defect existed in the same state at the time of sale. If the seller admits it, the buyer may return the slave. If the seller denies or claims a different state (e.g., before puberty vs. after), the buyer must further prove that the defect existed in that specific state at the seller's place. Only if the buyer fails to provide such evidence, and then requests an oath, the seller is made to swear that the slave never ran away at his place. The final section specifies the wording of the oath: the seller swears 'I sold it and delivered it and it never ran away at my place.' Alternative wordings are permissible, but the text warns against certain wordings that could prejudice the buyer's rights, particularly because a defect could arise between sale and delivery. The correct oath focuses on the seller's period of ownership. This detailed procedure ensures that the buyer's right to return the defective slave is protected while giving the seller a fair opportunity to defend.

Key Terms
اباق (abaq): The act of a slave running away from his master; considered a defect ('ayb) in sales.
بینہ (bayyinah): Clear evidence, usually two male witnesses or one male and two females, establishing a fact in court.
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's option to rescind the sale due to a defect present at the time of sale, provided certain conditions are met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer claims the slave he purchased ran away from the seller, but the slave has not run away since purchase. According to Hanafi law, can the buyer return the slave?
A) Yes, because the defect existed at the seller's place.
B) No, because the defect did not recur in the same state in the buyer's possession.
C) Yes, after the seller admits it.
D) No, only if the buyer presents witnesses from the seller's city.

Key Takeaway
The buyer must first prove that the slave ran away while in his own possession before the seller can be required to respond; the defect must also be in the same state as at the time of sale.

Proper Wording of the Seller's Oath in Defect Cases
Explains correct and incorrect oath wordings for seller in slave defect case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct and incorrect oath wordings for the seller in a sales defect dispute and explain the legal reason (talil) for the prohibition.

Core Idea
An oath must not deceive: the seller cannot swear in a way that obscures the buyer's right to return a slave that ran away after sale but before delivery.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a subtle but critical point in sales litigation: the precise wording of the seller's oath when the buyer proves a hidden defect—specifically, that the slave had run away. The judge must ensure the oath does not harm the buyer's right to return the slave. The author explains why two seemingly truthful wordings are impermissible, as they could mislead the court and deprive the buyer of his rightful claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute
The buyer claims the slave had a defect (flew) and proves it with witnesses. The seller must deny this via oath. Determine the timeframe: the defect could have occurred between sale and delivery.
The defect in question is 'abaq (running away), which is a hidden defect that gives the buyer khiyar al-'ayb.
2
Recognize Permissible Wordings
The text provides permissible wordings that explicitly cover the period from sale to delivery, e.g., 'By Allah, he never ran away from me until delivery.' These remove ambiguity.
Two permissible wordings are mentioned: one swearing that the slave never ran away 'until delivery', and another that the buyer has no right of return for that claim.
3
Recognize Impermissible Wordings
Two wordings are forbidden: (1) 'I sold him without defect' (only mentioning sale), and (2) 'I sold him and handed him over without defect' (implying defect absent at both times).
These wordings do not cover the gap between sale and delivery, where the defect could have arisen.
4
Understand the Legal Reason (Talil)
The prohibition is because a defect can occur after sale but before delivery, still giving the buyer the right to return. The forbidden wordings mislead listeners into thinking the buyer has no right, harming the buyer's interest.
The Hanafi principle is: 'Defect that appears after sale but before delivery also gives khiyar al-'ayb.'
5
Apply the Ruling
The judge must avoid the two forbidden wordings and use or instruct the seller to use the correct wording that covers the entire period until delivery, protecting the buyer's right.
This ruling is based on preventing deception and harm (darar).

Source Text

ترجمہ .. پس جب مشتری نے گواہ قائم کئے تو بائع کو الہ تعالیٰ کی قسم دلائی جائے گی کہ اس نے یہ غلام فروخت کیا اور مشتری کو سپر د کیا۔ حالانکہ وہ بائع کے پاس کبھی نہیں بھاگا۔ ایسا ہی کتاب میں کہا اور اگر چاہے تو قاضی بائع سے اللہ کی قسم اس طرح دلائے کہ مشتری کا تجھ پر حق واپسی اس وجہ سے نہیں ہے جس کا وہ مدعی ہے۔ یا بخدا یہ تیرے پاس کبھی نہیں بھاگا ہے۔ بہر حال قاضی ، بائع کو (اس طرح) قسم نہ دلائے کہ واللہ بائع نے اس غلام کو فروخت کیا حالانکہ اس میں یہ عیب نہیں تھا۔ اور نہ (اس طرح قسم دلائے ) کہ واللہ اس نے یہ غلام فروخت کیا اور اس کو سپرد کیا۔ حالانکہ اس میں عیب نہ تھا۔ کیونکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک صورت میں مشتری کا لحاظ ترک ہو جاتا ہے۔ کیونکہ عیب کبھی فروخت کرنے کے بعد (اور ) سپرد کرنے سے پہلے پیدا ہو جاتا ہے۔ اور یہ بھی واپسی کا موجب ہے۔ حالانکہ پہلی صورت میں اس سے غفلت ہے اور دوسری صورت میں وہم ہوتا ہے کہ عیب نہ ہونے کا تعلق دونوں شرطوں سے ہے۔ پس بائع قسم میں ( یہ ) تاویل کرے گا کہ عیب مبیع سپر د کرتے وقت موجود ہے لیکن فروخت کرتے وقت موجود نہیں تھا۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ جب مشتری نے گواہ قائم کر کے اپنے پاس غلام کا بھاگنا ثابت کر دیا تو اب بائع سے قسم لی جائے گی اور بائع تین طرح قسم کھا سکتا ہے۔ ا۔ یا تو یوں کہے کہ بخدا میں نے یہ غلام فروخت کیا اور مشتری کے سپرد کیا حالانکہ سپر دکرنے تک وہ میرے پاس کبھی نہیں بھاگا۔ یہی الفاظ
کتاب میں مذکور ہیں اور کتاب سے مراد مبسوط ہے اور بعض نے کہا کہ اس جگہ کتاب سے جامع صغیر مراد ہے۔
-۲ یایوں کہے خدا کی قسم مشتری کو میری طرف مبیع واپس کرنے کا حق اس وجہ سے ثابت نہیں جس کا وہ مدعی ہے۔ یا اس طرح قسم کے الفاظ ادا کرے۔ واللہ یہ غلام سپر د کرنے تک میرے پاس کبھی نہیں بھاگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ مندرجہ ذیل الفاظ کے ساتھ قسم نہ لی جائے۔
ا۔ بائع نے کہا بخدا میں نے یہ غلام فروخت کیا۔ حالانکہ اس میں یہ عیب نہیں تھا۔
-۲ یا کہا بخدا میں نے یہ غلام فروخت کیا اور مشتری کے سپرد کیا حالانکہ اس میں یہ عیب نہ تھا۔ ان دونوں صورتوں میں بائع کی قسم معتبر نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ مذکورہ دونوں صورتوں میں مشتری کا لحاظ ختم ہو جاتا ہے۔ اس طور پر کہ کبھی فروخت کرنے کے بعد اور مشتری کو سپرد کرنے سے پہلے مبیع کے اندر عیب پیدا ہو جاتا ہے اور اس عیب کی وجہ سے مشتری کو مبیع واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے۔ اور بائع نے کہا کہ واللہ میں نے اس کو فروخت کیا حالانکہ اس میں عیب نہیں تھا تو بائع کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ فروخت کرتے وقت عیب نہ تھا بلکہ بعد میں سپرد کرنے سے پہلے پیدا ہوا انیس بائع اپنی قسم میں سچا ہوا اور سننے والے اس منطق سے بے خبر رہے تو اس میں سراسر مشتری کا نقصان ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں صبح واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے لیکن بائع کی قسم سے غافل ہونے کی وجہ سے لوگوں نے یہ خیال کیا کہ بائع چونکہ قسم کھا گیا ۔ اسلئے مشتری کو بیچ واپس کرنے کا حق نہ ہوگا اور ظاہر ہے کہ اس میں مشتری کا نقصان ہے۔ اسلئے ان الفاظ کے ساتھ بائع کو قسم کھانے کی اجازت نہ دی جائے گی اور دوسری صورت میں جب بائع نے کہا واللہ میں نے اس کو فروخت کیا اور سپر د کیا حالانکہ اس میں عیب نہ تھا تو سننے والوں کو یہ وہم ہو گا کہ عیب نہ ہونے کا تعلق بیع اور تسلیم دونوں کے ساتھ ہے یعنی سننے والے ان الفاظ سے یہ سمجھیں گے کہ بائع کا مقصد یہ ہے کہ نہ فروخت کرتے وقت کوئی عیب تھا اور نہ سپر کرتے وقت کوئی عیب تھا اور اس میں مشتری کا سراسر نقصان ہے اسلئے ان الفاظ کیساتھ بھی بائع کو قسم کھانے کی اجازت نہ دی جائے گی۔
Translation: When the buyer establishes witnesses, the seller will be made to swear by Allah that he sold this slave and handed him over to the buyer, while the slave never ran away from the seller. [If he wishes, the judge may make the seller swear: 'The buyer has no right of return against you for the reason he claims' or 'By Allah, this slave never ran away from you.' However, the judge should not make the seller swear 'By Allah, the seller sold this slave while it had no such defect' nor 'By Allah, he sold this slave and handed him over while it had no defect.' Because in each of these cases the buyer's interest is ignored. For a defect may arise after the sale but before delivery, and that also gives the right of return. In the first case that is overlooked, and in the second case it is implied that the absence of defect pertains to both conditions. So the seller would interpret his oath as meaning that the defect was present at delivery but not at the time of sale. Explanation: When the buyer proves the slave's flight, the seller can swear in three ways: 1. 'By Allah, I sold this slave and handed him over, and he never ran away from me until delivery.' These are the words mentioned in the book (al-Mabsut or al-Jami' al-Saghir). 2. 'By Allah, the buyer has no right to return the sold item for the reason he claims' or 'By Allah, this slave never ran away from me until delivery.' The author of al-Hidayah says that the following wordings should not be used: a) The seller says 'By Allah, I sold this slave and he had no defect.' b) 'By Allah, I sold this slave and handed him over and he had no defect.' In both these cases the seller's oath is not valid because the buyer's interest is eliminated. For a defect may arise after sale but before delivery, and the buyer has the right to return, but the seller's oath would deceive listeners into thinking the buyer has no right. Therefore such wordings are not permitted.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from al-Hidayah explaining the correct procedure for the seller's oath when the buyer proves that the slave had a defect (specifically, having run away). The text begins by stating that when the buyer establishes witnesses, the seller must swear an oath that he sold the slave and handed him over, and that the slave never ran away from him. The judge has discretion to choose the exact wording, but must avoid two specific wordings. The first impermissible wording is: 'By Allah, I sold this slave and he had no defect.' The reason is that the defect could have arisen after the sale but before delivery, which would still give the buyer the right to return. The seller could be truthful in his oath if he meant that there was no defect at the time of sale, but the listener would misunderstand and think the buyer has no right. The second impermissible wording is: 'By Allah, I sold this slave and handed him over, and he had no defect.' This creates the impression that there was no defect at both times, but the seller could interpret it as referring only to the time of sale, while the defect might have occurred before delivery. In both cases, the buyer's right to return is harmed. Therefore, the judge must use the correct wording that explicitly covers the entire period until delivery, ensuring the buyer's right is protected. The text also mentions that the seller can swear in three acceptable ways, including swearing that the buyer has no right of return for the claimed reason. This ruling is based on the principle of preventing harm (darar) and avoiding deception in oaths.

Key Terms
بائع (ba'i'): Seller of the slave
مشتری (mushtari): Buyer of the slave
عیب ('ayb): Defect; specifically the slave having run away (إباق) in this context
إباق (abaq): Running away of a slave
يمين (yamin): Oath; specifically the seller's oath in court
تسلیم (taslim): Delivery of the sold item to the buyer

Checkpoint
Why is the seller not allowed to swear 'By Allah, I sold this slave and he had no defect'?
A) Because the seller might be lying.
B) Because the defect could have occurred after sale but before delivery, and the buyer still has the right to return, but the oath wording obscures that right.
C) Because the buyer must also swear.
D) Because the slave might have run away after delivery.

Key Takeaway
The seller's oath in a defect dispute must explicitly cover the period from sale to delivery to protect the buyer's right to return the goods if the defect arose after sale.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Ruling on Seller's Oath When Buyer Lacks Witnesses for Slave's Flight
Discusses ikhtilaf on buyer's right to demand oath without witnesses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the buyer's right to demand the seller's oath for a slave's flight defect and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Without witnesses, the buyer's claim is valid enough to demand the seller's oath according to the Sahibayn.

In sales disputes, when a buyer alleges a defect in the purchased slave but lacks witnesses, the question arises whether he can compel the seller to swear an oath denying knowledge of the defect. This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, the Sahibayn, and clarifies which view is relied upon in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The buyer claims the slave has a defect (tendency to run away) but has no witnesses to prove it.
2
State the ruling of the Sahibayn
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the buyer can demand the seller to swear an oath about his knowledge of the defect.
The text says 'يحلف على قولهما' (the seller swears according to the two).
3
Recognize the dispute regarding Abu Hanifa
The mashayikh differed on Abu Hanifa's view: some said he allows it, others said he does not.
This ikhtilaf is among later scholars.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
They argue that the buyer's claim is a valid legal claim because testimony (bayyinah) can be based on it; therefore, an oath (tahlif) can also be based on it.
Reasoning: what supports testimony supports oath.
5
Analyze the attributed opposing view
The opposing view holds that a valid claim requires a proper opponent (khasm), and the buyer becomes an opponent only after the defect is proven.
Thus, no oath can be demanded until the defect is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشتری بینہ عیب پر قائم نہ کر سکا وبائع سے قسم لینے کا ارادہ کیا تو قسم لی جائے گی یا نہیں
ولو لم يجد المشترى بينة على قيام العيب عنده واراد تحليف البائع بالله ما يعلم انه ابق عنده يحلف على قولهما واختلف المشائخ قولى ابى حنيفة لهما ان الدعوى معتبرة حتى يترتب عليها البنية فكذا يترتب التحليف وله على ماقاله البعض ان الحلف يترتب على دعوى صحيحة وليست تصح الا من خصم ولا يصير خصما فيه الابعد قيام العيب واذا نكل عن اليمين عندهما يحلف ثانيا للرد على الوجه الذي قد مناه قال رضى الله عنه اذا كان الدعوى فى اباق الكبير يحلف ما ابق منذ بلغ مبلغ الرجال لان الا باق في الصغر لا يوجب رده بعد البلوغ
ترجمہ اور اگر مشتری اپنے پاس عیب موجود ہونے پر گواہ موجود نہ کر سکا اور بائع سے ( ان الفاظ کے ساتھ قسم دلانے کا ارادہ کیا کہ واللہ وہ نہیں جانتا کہ یہ غلام مشتری کے پاس بھاگا ہے تو صاحبین کے قول پر بائع سے قسم لی جائے گی اور ابوحنیفہ کے قول پر مشائخ نے اختلاف کیا ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ دعوی معتبر ہے حتی کہ اس پر گواہی مرتب ہے اس لئے قسم لینا بھی مرتب ہوگا اور بعض مشائخ کے قول کے مطابق امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ دعوی صحیح پر تم کا ترتب ہوتا ہے اور دعوی صحیح نہیں ہوتا مگر خصم کی طرف سے اور مشتری اس دعوئی میں خصم نہیں ہوگا مگر ثبوت عیب کے بعد اور جب صاحبین کے نزدیک بائع نے قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا تو دوبارہ اس سے واپسی کے واسطے اس طور پر قسم لی جائے گی جس کو ہم نے پہلے بیان کیا۔ مصنف نے کہا کہ جب دعویٰ بالغ غلام کے بھاگنے میں ہو تو بائع قسم کھائے کہ جب سے وہ مردوں کی طرح بلوغ کے مرتبہ کو پہنچا کبھی میرے پاس سے نہیں بھاگا کیونکہ صغرسنی میں بھاگنا بالغ ہونے کے بعد واپسی کا موجب نہیں ہے۔ تشریح صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ اگر مشتری اپنے پاس وجود عیب پر گواہ پیش نہ کر سکا اور اس نے مطالبہ کیا کہ بائع یہ قسم کھائے کہ مجھ کو معلوم نہیں کہ یہ غلام مشتری کے پاس بھاگا ہے یا نہیں بھاگا یعنی بھاگنے کا عیب مشتری کے پاس ظاہر ہوا ہے یا نہیں ظاہر ہوا مجھے کچھ معلوم نہیں ہے۔ صاحبین کے نزدیک مشتری، بائع سے اس طرح کی قسم لے سکتا ہے۔ البتہ حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کے مذہب کے بارے میں مشائخ کا اختلاف ہے چنانچہ بعض مشائخ کا خیال ہے کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک بھی مشتری کو بائع سے قسم لینے کا حق ہے اور بعض نے کہا کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک مشتری کو یہ حق حاصل نہیں ہے۔
صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہےکہ مشتری کا غلام کے اندر عیب اباق کا دعوی صحیح اور معتبر ہے اور معتبر ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس پر شہادت کا ترتب ہوا ہے۔ چنانچہ عیب ثابت کرنے کیلئے مشتری سے گواہوں کا مطالبہ کیا گیا تھا اور جس پر شہادت کا ترتب ہوتا ہے اس پر قسم دلانا بھی مترتب ہوتا ہے اسلئے اس دعوئی پر قسم کی جا سکتی ہے۔ حاصل یہ کہ مشتری نے اگر اپنے اس دعوئی پر گواہ پیش کئے تو ان کی گواہی قبول کی
Translation: If the buyer does not find witnesses for the existence of the defect with him and intends to make the seller swear by Allah 'he does not know that it ran away while with him', then according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the seller swears. The mashayikh differed regarding the saying of Abu Hanifa. Their (the two's) proof: the claim is valid such that testimony is based on it, so the oath is also based on it. According to what some said regarding him (Abu Hanifa): the oath is based on a valid claim, and a claim is only valid from an opponent (khasm), and the buyer does not become an opponent in this except after the establishment of the defect. If he (the seller) refuses the oath according to the two, he swears a second time for the return in the manner we have mentioned. The author (may Allah be pleased with him) said: If the claim is regarding the flight of an adult (slave), he swears: 'He did not run away since reaching the age of manhood' because flight in childhood does not necessitate his return after adulthood. Explanation: If the buyer cannot produce witnesses for the existence of the defect with him and demands that the seller swear 'I do not know that this slave ran away while with the buyer,' then according to the two (Sahibayn) the buyer can demand this oath. However, regarding Imam Abu Hanifa's school, the mashayikh differed: some say that according to Imam Abu Hanifa the buyer also has the right to demand the oath, and others say that according to him the buyer does not have this right. The proof of the two: the buyer's claim that the slave has the defect of flight is valid and reliable, and the proof of its reliability is that testimony is based on it. Since witnesses were demanded from the buyer to prove the defect, and what testimony is based on can also be the basis for an oath, therefore an oath can be administered on this claim. In summary, if the buyer produces witnesses on his claim, their testimony is accepted.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the buyer can demand the seller to swear an oath about his knowledge of the slave's flight defect when the buyer lacks witnesses. The text begins with the ruling according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani): the seller does swear. Then it notes that the mashayikh differed about Imam Abu Hanifa's position. The Sahibayn's reasoning is that the buyer's claim is a valid legal claim (da'wa sahiha) because it can be supported by testimony (bayyina). Since testimony is based on the claim, so is the oath a means of proof. Therefore the buyer can demand an oath. Regarding Abu Hanifa, some later scholars said he also allows it, while others said he does not because the claim is only valid when the opponent (khasm) has been established, i.e., after the defect is proven. The buyer does not become an opponent until the defect is established. The text also discusses the case of a slave who ran away as a child: the seller only swears about flight after the slave reached majority, because flight in childhood does not give the right of return after adulthood. This shows the precision of the Hanafi school in limiting the scope of the oath to what is legally relevant. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) appears to be that of the Sahibayn, as the Hidayah presents their view as the operative ruling (يحلف على قولهما).

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Witnesses or evidence proving the defect
تحليف (tahlif): Administering an oath to the opponent
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath
خصم (khasm): Opponent in a lawsuit
دعوى (da'wa): Legal claim by the buyer that the slave has a defect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why can the buyer demand an oath from the seller even without witnesses?
A) Because the seller is always required to swear in disputes.
B) Because the claim itself is valid and can be supported by testimony, so it can also be supported by an oath.
C) Because the buyer has the burden of proof.
D) Because the judge has discretion to order oaths.

Key Takeaway
The Sahibayn's view that a valid claim without witnesses can still trigger the seller's oath is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Buyer's Right to Demand an Oath Without Evidence
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath demand without evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples on whether a buyer who cannot produce evidence for a defect can demand an oath from the seller, and explain the proof for each position.

Core Idea
An oath requires a valid claim from a litigant; without evidence, the buyer is not yet a litigant.

When a buyer discovers a defect but cannot prove it, can he force the seller to swear ignorance? This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah records a fascinating intra-Hanafi debate between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples. The disagreement hinges on the nature of a legal claim and the role of evidence and oaths in litigation. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of procedural fiqh and the conditions for using oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Factual Scenario
The buyer finds a defect (absconding) in the purchased slave after taking possession, but cannot produce witnesses (bayyinah) that the defect existed while in his possession. He wants to make the seller swear that he has no knowledge of the absconding.
The defect is 'abaq' (running away). The buyer lacks formal evidence.
2
State the Position of the Two Disciples (Sahibayn)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the buyer can demand the oath. Their reasoning: the buyer's claim (da'wa) of the defect is valid — it would support testimony if evidence were available — so it also supports an oath.
Sahibayn treat da'wa as the foundation for both bayyinah and yamin.
3
State Imam Abu Hanifa's Position
According to one transmission, Abu Hanifa does not allow the oath. He argues that an oath requires a valid claim (da'wa sahiha) from a litigant (khasm). The buyer does not become a litigant until he first proves the defect through evidence (bayyinah). Since he has no evidence, he is not a litigant, his claim is not valid, and no oath can be administered.
There is a second transmission where Abu Hanifa agrees with the Sahibayn; scholars (mashayikh) differed on which is authentic.
4
Trace the Proof for Each Position
Sahibayn: 'The claim is valid such that testimony is based upon it, so the oath is also based upon it.' Abu Hanifa: 'The oath is based on a valid claim, and a valid claim only comes from a litigant; one becomes a litigant only after establishing the defect.' The text then contrasts bayyinah (which does not require a prior claim) with yamin (which does).
This distinction is key: bayyinah can be accepted without a claim (e.g., hudud), but yamin requires a prior litigant.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Under the Sahibayn's view, the buyer can force the seller to swear ignorance; if the seller refuses or swears, the case resolves. Under Abu Hanifa's view (one transmission), the buyer cannot demand the oath, so the case may be dismissed unless the buyer finds other evidence.
The text suggests the Sahibayn's view is operative in the procedure described later.

بائع یہ تاویل کرے گا کہ اجتماعی طور پر ان دونوں حالتوں میں عیب نہ تھا بلکہ سپر د کرتے وقت عیب تھا۔ اگر چہ فروخت کرتے وقت عیب نہ تھا۔ حالانکہ اس صورت میں مشتری کو بیچ والوں کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے لیکن بائع کی قسم سے دھو کہ کھا کر مشتری کا یہ اختیار سلب ہو گیا اور اس میں مشتری کا سراسر نقصان ہے اسلئے ان الفاظ کیساتھ بھی بائع کو قسم کھانے کی اجازت نہ دی جائے گی۔

امام صاحب کے قول کی دلیل ان مشائخ کے قول کی بناء پر جو مشتری کو بائع سے قسم لینے کا مجاز قرار نہیں دیتے یہ ہے کہ قسم کا ترتب دعوی صحیحہ پر ہوتا ہے اور دعوئی صحیح ہوتا ہے خصم کی طرف سے اور مشتری مذکورہ مسئلہ میں خصم اس وقت ہو سکتا ہے جبکہ وہ اپنے پاس عیب یعنی غلام کا بھا گنا دلیل شرعی شہادت سے ثابت کر دے حالانکہ مشتری ثبوت عیب پر بعینہ پیش نہ کر سکا تو مشتری خصم نہ ہو گا تو اس کی طرف سے دعوی بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا اور جب دعوی صحیح نہیں ہوا تو اس پر قسم کا ترتب بھی نہ ہوگا۔ اسلئے ہم نے کہا کہ حضرت امام صاحب کے نزدیک مشتری کو بائع سے قسم لینے کا کوئی حق نہیں ہے۔ اور صاحبین کا یہ کہنا کہ جس پر بینہ کا تر تب ہوتا ہے اس پر قسم کا بھی ترتب ہو گا غلط ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے وکیل ہونے کا دعویٰ کیا تو اس سے وکالت ثابت کرنے کیلئے بینہ کا مطالبہ تو ہو گا لیکن اگر یہ اپنی وکالت پر بینہ پیش نہ کر سکا تو منکر وکالت سے قسم نہیں لی جائے گی اور رہا بینہ تو اس کیلئے نفس دعوی ہی شرط نہیں ہے۔ چہ جائیکہ صحت دعوئی شرط ہو بلکہ بعینہ ان چیزوں پر بھی قائم ہوتا ہے جہاں سرے سے دعوی ہی نہ ہو مثلاً حدود میں ہے کہ حد زنا وغیرہ پر گواہی قبول کرنے کیلئے یہ شرط نہیں ہے کہ پہلے کوئی دعوی کرے بلکہ بغیر دعوی کے اگر شریعت کے مطابق شہادت میسر آگئی تو حد زنا جاری کر دی جائے گی۔ اس کے برخلاف قسم ہر قسم کیلئے جوئی صحیحہ کا ہونا ضروری ہے بغیر دعوی صحیحہ کے قسم کا ترتب نہ ہوگا۔ قسم اور بینہ میں فرق یہ ہے کہ قسم مشروع ہوتی ہے خصومت اور جھگڑا ختم کرنے کیلئے۔ پس قسم دلائے جانے سے پہلے قسم کا ہونا ضروری ہے اور مسئلہ مذکورہ میں مشتری شخصم اس وقت ہوگا جبکہ وہ اپنے پاس عیب ثابت کر دے حالانکہ اس نے عیب ثابت نہیں کیا ہے۔
حاصل ... یہ ہوا کہ اس مسئلہ میں مشتری خصم نہیں ہوا تو اس کی طرف سے دعوی بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا اور جب مشتری کی طرف سے دعوی صحیح نہ ہوا تو اس پر قسم بھی مترتب نہ ہوگی یعنی مشتری بائع سے قسم لینے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا اور مبینہ خصم ہونے کو ثابت کرنے کیلئے مشروع ہوا ہے پس بیز اس کا تقاضا نہیں کرتا کہ بینہ پیش کرنے والا بعینہ پیش کرنے سے پہلے خصم ہوا ۔ البتہ قسم دلانا اس بات کا مقتضی ہے کہ قسم دلانے والا پہلے
سے خصم ہو۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ صاحبین کے مذہب پر جب بائع نے اپنی لاعلمی کی قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا تو اب بائع سے دوبارہ اس طریقہ پر قسم لی جائے گی جو شروع مسئلہ میں بیان کیا گیا ہے یعنی جب بائع نے لاعلمی کی قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا تو اس کے انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے مشتری کے پاس عیب ثابت ہو گیا اور مشتری چونکہ اس عیب کی وجہ سے مبیع واپس کرے گا اسلئے اب دوبارہ بائع اس پر تم کھائے گا کہ واللہ میں نے اس غلام کو بیچا اور سپرد کیا حالانکہ میرے پاس یہ غلام کبھی نہیں بھا گا یعنی یہ عیب کبھی پیدا نہیں ہوایا اس کے ہم معنی جو دو طریقے کتاب میں مذکور ہیں ان کے ساتھ قسم کھائے اگر بائع اپنے پاس عیب موجود نہ ہونے پر قسم کھا گیا تو مشتری کو نام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا کیونکہ عیب حادث اور عیب جدید کی وجہ سے مشتری مبیع واپس کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہوتا۔ ہاں اگر بائع نے یہ قسم کھانے سے بھی انکار کر دیا تو اب مشتری غلام واپس کر سکتا ہے کیونکہ انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے عیب بائع کے پاس بھی ثابت ہو گیا ہے اور جو عیب بائع کے پاس پیدا ہوا ہو اس کی وجہ سے مبیع واپس کی جاسکتی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ کہتے ہیں کہ مشتری نے بھگوڑا ہونے کا دعوئی اگر بالغ غلام کے بارے میں کیا تو بائع اس طرح قسم کھائے کہ بالغ
The seller will interpret that the defect was not present in both states collectively, but rather at the time of delivery, although at the time of sale there was no defect. However, in this case the buyer has the option to return, but by the seller's oath he deceived and the buyer's option was removed, and there is complete loss for the buyer, so the seller will not be allowed to take an oath with these words either. The buyer could not establish evidence of the defect and intended to make the seller swear: is the oath taken or not? And if the buyer does not find evidence that the defect existed with him, and he intends to make the seller swear by Allah that he does not know that it absconded with him, he (the seller) swears according to their (the two disciples) statement. And the scholars differ regarding the statement of Abu Hanifa. For them (the two disciples), the claim is valid such that evidence is based upon it, so likewise the oath is based upon it. And for him (Abu Hanifa) according to what some said, the oath is based upon a valid claim, and it is only valid from a litigant, and one does not become a litigant in it except after establishment of the defect. And if he (the seller) refuses the oath according to them (the two disciples), he swears a second time for return in the manner that we have mentioned. He (the author) said: if the claim is regarding the absconding of an adult, he swears that it did not abscond since it reached the age of puberty, because absconding in childhood does not necessitate its return after puberty. Translation: And if the buyer could not produce witnesses for the existence of the defect with him, and he intended to make the seller swear (with these words: by Allah he does not know that this slave absconded at the buyer's), then according to the statement of the two disciples, the oath will be taken from the seller. And regarding Abu Hanifa's statement, the scholars differed. The proof of the two disciples is that the claim is valid, such that testimony is based upon it, so taking an oath will also be based upon it. And according to some scholars, the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the oath is based on a valid claim, and a valid claim is only from a litigant, and the buyer does not become a litigant in this claim except after proof of the defect. And when according to the two disciples the seller refused to take the oath, then a second oath will be taken from him for return in the manner we previously explained. The author said: when the claim is regarding the absconding of an adult slave, the seller swears that since he reached the age of puberty he never absconded from me, because absconding in childhood does not necessitate return after puberty. Explanation: The author of Hidayah said: if the buyer could not produce witnesses for the existence of the defect with him, and he demanded that the seller take an oath that 'I do not know whether this slave absconded at the buyer's or not', i.e., I have no knowledge whether the defect of absconding appeared at the buyer's or not. According to the two disciples, the buyer can take such an oath from the seller. However, regarding the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa, there is disagreement among the scholars: some scholars think that according to Imam Abu Hanifa the buyer also has the right to take an oath from the seller, and some said that according to Imam Abu Hanifa the buyer does not have this right. The proof of the two disciples is that the buyer's claim of the defect of absconding in the slave is correct and valid, and the proof of its validity is that testimony is based upon it. Thus, witnesses were demanded from the buyer to prove the defect, and on that which testimony is based, making an oath is also based, so an oath can be taken on this claim. The result is that if the buyer produced witnesses on his claim, their testimony will be accepted, and if the buyer could not produce witnesses, then the oath will be taken from the seller with the mentioned words, i.e., the seller will say: by Allah, I do not know about this slave's absconding at the buyer's. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa's statement, based on the statement of those scholars who do not allow the buyer to take an oath from the seller, is that the oath is based upon a valid claim, and a valid claim is from a litigant. In the mentioned issue, the buyer can become a litigant only when he proves the defect i.e., the absconding of the slave, through legal evidence (shahadah). But since the buyer could not produce evidence for the defect, the buyer will not be a litigant, so his claim will also not be valid, and when the claim is not valid, no oath will be based upon it. Therefore we said that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer has no right to take an oath from the seller. And what the two disciples say—that on which evidence is based, oath is also based—is incorrect. For example, if someone claims to be an agent, he will be required to produce evidence to prove the agency, but if he cannot produce evidence for his agency, the denial of agency will not be taken an oath from him. As for evidence, the mere claim itself is not a condition for it, let alone the validity of the claim being a condition; rather, evidence is established on things where there is no claim at all, such as in hudud: for accepting testimony on the hadd of zina etc., it is not a condition that a claim be made first; rather, without a claim, if legal testimony is available, the hadd of zina will be carried out. In contrast, for an oath, a valid claim is necessary; without a valid claim, an oath will not be based. The difference between oath and evidence is that the oath is legislated to end litigation and dispute. So before an oath is administered, the existence of the oath is necessary, and in the mentioned issue, the buyer becomes a litigant only when he proves the defect with him, whereas he did not prove the defect. The conclusion is that in this issue, the buyer did not become a litigant, so his claim will not be valid, and when the claim from the buyer is not valid, then an oath will not be based on it, meaning the buyer is not permitted to take an oath from the seller. And evidence is legislated to prove being a litigant, so evidence does not require that the person producing evidence be a litigant before producing evidence. However, making an oath requires that the person demanding the oath be a litigant beforehand. The author of Hidayah says: According to the madhhab of the two disciples, when the seller refused to take the oath of ignorance, then a second oath will be taken from the seller in the manner described at the beginning of the issue, i.e., when the seller refused to take the oath of ignorance, by his refusal the defect is proven with the buyer, and since the buyer will return the sold item due to this defect, now the seller will again take an oath on it: by Allah, I sold this slave and delivered him while this slave never absconded from me, meaning this defect never occurred. He will take an oath with the two methods that are synonymous and mentioned in the book. If the seller takes an oath that the defect did not exist with him, then the buyer will not have the option to return, because due to a later defect and a new defect, the buyer is not permitted to return the sold item. Yes, if the seller also refuses to take this oath, then now the buyer can return the slave, because by the refusal, the defect is proven with the seller as well, and due to a defect that occurred with the seller, the sold item can be returned. The author of Hidayah says: if the buyer made a claim of absconding regarding an adult slave, then the seller will swear in such a way that [the adult?... the text cuts off]
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with a specific legal scenario: a buyer discovers a defect (absconding, 'abaq) in a purchased slave after possession, but cannot produce witnesses to prove that the defect existed while the slave was in his possession. The buyer then wants to make the seller swear that he has no knowledge of the slave absconding while in the buyer's custody. The passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whether such an oath can be demanded.

The text first states the ruling of the Sahibayn (the two disciples): they allow the buyer to make the seller swear that he does not know about the absconding. Their reasoning is that the buyer's claim (da'wa) of the defect is valid and recognized by the law, as evidenced by the fact that if he could produce witnesses (bayyinah), their testimony would be accepted. Since the claim is valid enough to support testimony, it is also valid enough to support an oath (yamin).

Regarding Imam Abu Hanifa, the passage notes that later scholars (mashayikh) differed on his position. Some transmitted that he agrees with the Sahibayn, while others maintain that he does not allow the oath. The text gives the proof for Abu Hanifa's position: an oath requires a valid claim (da'wa sahiha), and a valid claim can only come from a litigant (khasm). The buyer does not become a litigant until he first establishes the defect through legal evidence. Since he failed to produce evidence, he is not a litigant, his claim is not valid, and thus no oath can be administered. The passage then highlights a key distinction between bayyinah (evidence) and yamin (oath). Bayyinah does not require a prior valid claim; it can even be accepted without any claim, as in hudud cases where witnesses come forward without a plaintiff. But an oath is specifically legislated to settle litigation, so it requires that the person demanding the oath already be a litigant. The buyer only becomes a litigant after proving the defect.

The text then discusses the procedure under the Sahibayn's view if the seller refuses the initial oath of ignorance. In that case, the seller's refusal effectively proves the defect existed with the buyer, entitling the buyer to return the slave. But then a second oath is administered to determine whether the defect existed at the time of sale (i.e., while the slave was with the seller). If the seller swears that the slave never absconded while with him, then the defect is considered to have arisen after sale (a new defect), and the buyer cannot return the slave due to a new defect (since the buyer could have discovered it earlier). If the seller refuses this second oath, the defect is deemed to have existed at the time of sale, and the buyer can return the slave.

Finally, the passage addresses the specific wording for an adult slave: the seller swears that the slave has not absconded since reaching puberty, because absconding during childhood does not count as a defect affecting return after majority. The text ends abruptly mid-sentence.

Key Terms
خصم (khasm): A litigant or party to a legal dispute; one who has standing to make a claim or demand an oath.
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or assertion of a right, such as claiming a defect in a purchased item.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically the testimony of two qualified witnesses; proof of a claim.
يمين (yamin): An oath taken to resolve a dispute when evidence is lacking; a procedural tool in litigation.
اباق (abaq): Absconding or running away of a slave; a defect that gives the buyer the right to return the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why can the buyer demand an oath from the seller even without evidence?
A) Because the seller is always a defendant.
B) Because the buyer's claim is valid and supports evidence, so it also supports an oath.
C) Because the defect is obvious.
D) Because the Quran commands oaths in all transactions.

Key Takeaway
The Sahibayn allow the oath demand based on the strength of the claim; Abu Hanifa requires the buyer to first become a litigant through evidence, reflecting a key procedural distinction.

The Distinction Between Bayyinah and Yamin and the Second Oath Procedure
Explains distinction between bayyinah and yamin and the second oath procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between bayyinah (evidence) and yamin (oath), and describe the second oath procedure when the seller refuses the first oath.

Core Idea
Bayyinah can be accepted without a prior valid claim; yamin requires a valid claim from a litigant.

The previous lesson presented the dispute on oath demand. The text then provides a powerful critique of the Sahibayn's analogy by distinguishing bayyinah from yamin. This distinction is foundational for Hanafi procedural law. Additionally, the passage describes what happens if the seller refuses the oath: a second oath is administered to determine whether the defect existed at the time of sale. Understanding this procedure is essential for any student of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Sahibayn's Analogy
The Sahibayn argued that since the claim supports testimony, it also supports an oath.
This analogy is being critiqued in this part of the text.
2
Identify the Critique: Bayyinah vs. Yamin
The text argues that bayyinah does not require a prior claim or litigant status; it can be accepted even without any claim, as in hudud. In contrast, yamin is legislated to end disputes, so it requires a valid claim from a litigant. The Sahibayn's analogy fails because it assumes the same precondition for both.
The hudud example: testimony for zina is accepted without a plaintiff. The agency example: an agent who cannot prove agency does not get an oath from the principal's denial.
3
Analyze the Agency Counterexample
If someone claims to be an agent, he must produce evidence of agency. If he fails, the principal (denying agency) is not made to swear. Evidence (bayyinah) would have been required to prove agency, but the lack of evidence does not trigger an oath.
This shows that evidential support and oath support are not co-extensive.
4
Understand the Second Oath Procedure
According to the Sahibayn's view, if the seller refuses the first oath (of ignorance), his refusal proves the defect existed with the buyer. Then a second oath is administered to determine whether the defect existed at the time of sale or arose after. If the seller swears the defect was not present during his ownership, it is considered a new defect, and the buyer cannot return the item. If the seller refuses, the defect is deemed pre-existing, and the buyer may return the item.
The second oath is crucial to establish liability.
5
Note the Adult Slave Condition
If the claim is about the absconding of an adult slave, the seller swears that the slave has not absconded since reaching puberty. Absconding in childhood does not affect the return after majority.
The oath wording is specific to the age of the slave.

حاصل ... یہ ہوا کہ اس مسئلہ میں مشتری خصم نہیں ہوا تو اس کی طرف سے دعوی بھی صحیح نہ ہوگا اور جب مشتری کی طرف سے دعوی صحیح نہ ہوا تو اس پر قسم بھی مترتب نہ ہوگی یعنی مشتری بائع سے قسم لینے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا اور مبینہ خصم ہونے کو ثابت کرنے کیلئے مشروع ہوا ہے پس بیز اس کا تقاضا نہیں کرتا کہ بینہ پیش کرنے والا بعینہ پیش کرنے سے پہلے خصم ہوا ۔ البتہ قسم دلانا اس بات کا مقتضی ہے کہ قسم دلانے والا پہلے
سے خصم ہو۔
صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ صاحبین کے مذہب پر جب بائع نے اپنی لاعلمی کی قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا تو اب بائع سے دوبارہ اس طریقہ پر قسم لی جائے گی جو شروع مسئلہ میں بیان کیا گیا ہے یعنی جب بائع نے لاعلمی کی قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا تو اس کے انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے مشتری کے پاس عیب ثابت ہو گیا اور مشتری چونکہ اس عیب کی وجہ سے مبیع واپس کرے گا اسلئے اب دوبارہ بائع اس پر تم کھائے گا کہ واللہ میں نے اس غلام کو بیچا اور سپرد کیا حالانکہ میرے پاس یہ غلام کبھی نہیں بھا گا یعنی یہ عیب کبھی پیدا نہیں ہوایا اس کے ہم معنی جو دو طریقے کتاب میں مذکور ہیں ان کے ساتھ قسم کھائے اگر بائع اپنے پاس عیب موجود نہ ہونے پر قسم کھا گیا تو مشتری کو نام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہوگا کیونکہ عیب حادث اور عیب جدید کی وجہ سے مشتری مبیع واپس کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہوتا۔ ہاں اگر بائع نے یہ قسم کھانے سے بھی انکار کر دیا تو اب مشتری غلام واپس کر سکتا ہے کیونکہ انکار کرنے کی وجہ سے عیب بائع کے پاس بھی ثابت ہو گیا ہے اور جو عیب بائع کے پاس پیدا ہوا ہو اس کی وجہ سے مبیع واپس کی جاسکتی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ کہتے ہیں کہ مشتری نے بھگوڑا ہونے کا دعوئی اگر بالغ غلام کے بارے میں کیا تو بائع اس طرح قسم کھائے کہ بالغ
The seller will interpret that the defect was not present in both states collectively, but rather at the time of delivery, although at the time of sale there was no defect. However, in this case the buyer has the option to return, but by the seller's oath he deceived and the buyer's option was removed, and there is complete loss for the buyer, so the seller will not be allowed to take an oath with these words either. The buyer could not establish evidence of the defect and intended to make the seller swear: is the oath taken or not? And if the buyer does not find evidence that the defect existed with him, and he intends to make the seller swear by Allah that he does not know that it absconded with him, he (the seller) swears according to their (the two disciples) statement. And the scholars differ regarding the statement of Abu Hanifa. For them (the two disciples), the claim is valid such that evidence is based upon it, so likewise the oath is based upon it. And for him (Abu Hanifa) according to what some said, the oath is based upon a valid claim, and it is only valid from a litigant, and one does not become a litigant in it except after establishment of the defect. And if he (the seller) refuses the oath according to them (the two disciples), he swears a second time for return in the manner that we have mentioned. He (the author) said: if the claim is regarding the absconding of an adult, he swears that it did not abscond since it reached the age of puberty, because absconding in childhood does not necessitate its return after puberty. Translation: And if the buyer could not produce witnesses for the existence of the defect with him, and he intended to make the seller swear (with these words: by Allah he does not know that this slave absconded at the buyer's), then according to the statement of the two disciples, the oath will be taken from the seller. And regarding Abu Hanifa's statement, the scholars differed. The proof of the two disciples is that the claim is valid, such that testimony is based upon it, so taking an oath will also be based upon it. And according to some scholars, the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the oath is based on a valid claim, and a valid claim is only from a litigant, and the buyer does not become a litigant in this claim except after proof of the defect. And when according to the two disciples the seller refused to take the oath, then a second oath will be taken from him for return in the manner we previously explained. The author said: when the claim is regarding the absconding of an adult slave, the seller swears that since he reached the age of puberty he never absconded from me, because absconding in childhood does not necessitate return after puberty. Explanation: The author of Hidayah said: if the buyer could not produce witnesses for the existence of the defect with him, and he demanded that the seller take an oath that 'I do not know whether this slave absconded at the buyer's or not', i.e., I have no knowledge whether the defect of absconding appeared at the buyer's or not. According to the two disciples, the buyer can take such an oath from the seller. However, regarding the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa, there is disagreement among the scholars: some scholars think that according to Imam Abu Hanifa the buyer also has the right to take an oath from the seller, and some said that according to Imam Abu Hanifa the buyer does not have this right. The proof of the two disciples is that the buyer's claim of the defect of absconding in the slave is correct and valid, and the proof of its validity is that testimony is based upon it. Thus, witnesses were demanded from the buyer to prove the defect, and on that which testimony is based, making an oath is also based, so an oath can be taken on this claim. The result is that if the buyer produced witnesses on his claim, their testimony will be accepted, and if the buyer could not produce witnesses, then the oath will be taken from the seller with the mentioned words, i.e., the seller will say: by Allah, I do not know about this slave's absconding at the buyer's. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa's statement, based on the statement of those scholars who do not allow the buyer to take an oath from the seller, is that the oath is based upon a valid claim, and a valid claim is from a litigant. In the mentioned issue, the buyer can become a litigant only when he proves the defect i.e., the absconding of the slave, through legal evidence (shahadah). But since the buyer could not produce evidence for the defect, the buyer will not be a litigant, so his claim will also not be valid, and when the claim is not valid, no oath will be based upon it. Therefore we said that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer has no right to take an oath from the seller. And what the two disciples say—that on which evidence is based, oath is also based—is incorrect. For example, if someone claims to be an agent, he will be required to produce evidence to prove the agency, but if he cannot produce evidence for his agency, the denial of agency will not be taken an oath from him. As for evidence, the mere claim itself is not a condition for it, let alone the validity of the claim being a condition; rather, evidence is established on things where there is no claim at all, such as in hudud: for accepting testimony on the hadd of zina etc., it is not a condition that a claim be made first; rather, without a claim, if legal testimony is available, the hadd of zina will be carried out. In contrast, for an oath, a valid claim is necessary; without a valid claim, an oath will not be based. The difference between oath and evidence is that the oath is legislated to end litigation and dispute. So before an oath is administered, the existence of the oath is necessary, and in the mentioned issue, the buyer becomes a litigant only when he proves the defect with him, whereas he did not prove the defect. The conclusion is that in this issue, the buyer did not become a litigant, so his claim will not be valid, and when the claim from the buyer is not valid, then an oath will not be based on it, meaning the buyer is not permitted to take an oath from the seller. And evidence is legislated to prove being a litigant, so evidence does not require that the person producing evidence be a litigant before producing evidence. However, making an oath requires that the person demanding the oath be a litigant beforehand. The author of Hidayah says: According to the madhhab of the two disciples, when the seller refused to take the oath of ignorance, then a second oath will be taken from the seller in the manner described at the beginning of the issue, i.e., when the seller refused to take the oath of ignorance, by his refusal the defect is proven with the buyer, and since the buyer will return the sold item due to this defect, now the seller will again take an oath on it: by Allah, I sold this slave and delivered him while this slave never absconded from me, meaning this defect never occurred. He will take an oath with the two methods that are synonymous and mentioned in the book. If the seller takes an oath that the defect did not exist with him, then the buyer will not have the option to return, because due to a later defect and a new defect, the buyer is not permitted to return the sold item. Yes, if the seller also refuses to take this oath, then now the buyer can return the slave, because by the refusal, the defect is proven with the seller as well, and due to a defect that occurred with the seller, the sold item can be returned. The author of Hidayah says: if the buyer made a claim of absconding regarding an adult slave, then the seller will swear in such a way that [the adult?... the text cuts off]
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah deals with a specific legal scenario: a buyer discovers a defect (absconding, 'abaq) in a purchased slave after possession, but cannot produce witnesses to prove that the defect existed while the slave was in his possession. The buyer then wants to make the seller swear that he has no knowledge of the slave absconding while in the buyer's custody. The passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whether such an oath can be demanded.

The text first states the ruling of the Sahibayn (the two disciples): they allow the buyer to make the seller swear that he does not know about the absconding. Their reasoning is that the buyer's claim (da'wa) of the defect is valid and recognized by the law, as evidenced by the fact that if he could produce witnesses (bayyinah), their testimony would be accepted. Since the claim is valid enough to support testimony, it is also valid enough to support an oath (yamin).

Regarding Imam Abu Hanifa, the passage notes that later scholars (mashayikh) differed on his position. Some transmitted that he agrees with the Sahibayn, while others maintain that he does not allow the oath. The text gives the proof for Abu Hanifa's position: an oath requires a valid claim (da'wa sahiha), and a valid claim can only come from a litigant (khasm). The buyer does not become a litigant until he first establishes the defect through legal evidence. Since he failed to produce evidence, he is not a litigant, his claim is not valid, and thus no oath can be administered. The passage then highlights a key distinction between bayyinah (evidence) and yamin (oath). Bayyinah does not require a prior valid claim; it can even be accepted without any claim, as in hudud cases where witnesses come forward without a plaintiff. But an oath is specifically legislated to settle litigation, so it requires that the person demanding the oath already be a litigant. The buyer only becomes a litigant after proving the defect.

The text then discusses the procedure under the Sahibayn's view if the seller refuses the initial oath of ignorance. In that case, the seller's refusal effectively proves the defect existed with the buyer, entitling the buyer to return the slave. But then a second oath is administered to determine whether the defect existed at the time of sale (i.e., while the slave was with the seller). If the seller swears that the slave never absconded while with him, then the defect is considered to have arisen after sale (a new defect), and the buyer cannot return the slave due to a new defect (since the buyer could have discovered it earlier). If the seller refuses this second oath, the defect is deemed to have existed at the time of sale, and the buyer can return the slave.

Finally, the passage addresses the specific wording for an adult slave: the seller swears that the slave has not absconded since reaching puberty, because absconding during childhood does not count as a defect affecting return after majority. The text ends abruptly mid-sentence.

Key Terms
خصم (khasm): A litigant or party to a legal dispute; one who has standing to make a claim or demand an oath.
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or assertion of a right, such as claiming a defect in a purchased item.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically the testimony of two qualified witnesses; proof of a claim.
يمين (yamin): An oath taken to resolve a dispute when evidence is lacking; a procedural tool in litigation.
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath; in Hanafi law, nukul can lead to the case being decided against the refusing party.
اباق (abaq): Absconding or running away of a slave; a defect that gives the buyer the right to return the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn's view, what happens if the seller refuses to take the first oath (of ignorance)?
A) The case is dismissed.
B) The buyer can immediately return the item.
C) A second oath is administered to determine whether the defect existed at the time of sale.
D) The seller is forced to take the first oath.

Key Takeaway
Bayyinah and yamin have different prerequisites: bayyinah works without a claim, yamin requires a valid claim from a litigant. The second oath procedure ensures fairness by determining the timing of the defect.

Buyer's Right to Demand Oath: Imam Abu Hanifa's Position
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath in sale of defective slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Abu Hanifa's position on the buyer's right to demand an oath from the seller in a sale of a defective slave, and explain the basis for the disagreement with the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A buyer cannot demand an oath without first proving the defect, because an oath requires an opponent's valid claim.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a buyer discovers a defect in a purchased slave but cannot produce witnesses, may he demand an oath from the seller? Imam Abu Hanifa holds that he may not, while the two Sahibayn argue otherwise. We will explore the reasoning behind each position, focusing on the critical distinction between evidence (bayyinah) and oath (qasam).

Guided Path
1
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer cannot demand an oath from the seller because the buyer has not proven the defect with evidence.
The key condition: a valid oath requires a valid claim from an opponent.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's opposing view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the buyer can demand an oath, reasoning that wherever evidence can be required, an oath can also be required.
They equate the legal function of evidence and oath in this context.
3
Analyze Imam Abu Hanifa's dalil
The Imam argues that an oath is contingent on a valid claim (da‘wa sahiha), which requires an opponent (khasm). The buyer is not an opponent until he proves the defect legally.
The opponent status is established only by presenting evidence.
4
Examine the Hanafi response to Sahibayn
The author refutes the Sahibayn's analogy by giving the example of a claimant of agency (wakalah): such a claimant must provide evidence, but if he fails, the denier is not made to take an oath. Further, evidence can be accepted without any claim (e.g., hudud), while oath always requires a prior claim.
Evidence proves facts; oath ends disputes. They have different preconditions.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Imam Abu Hanifa, the seller is not required to swear if the buyer lacks evidence. Under Sahibayn, the seller must swear if the buyer demands it, even without evidence, with consequences for refusal.
The passage later describes the procedure under Sahibayn's view, indicating it is operative despite the disagreement.

Chapter of Transactions. Ashraf al-Hidayah commentary Urdu Hidayah Volume Eight. It will be, and if the buyer cannot produce witnesses, then the seller will be made to take an oath with the mentioned words. That is, the seller will say: 'By Allah, I do not know of this slave's running away at the buyer's place.' The evidence for Imam Sahib's statement, based on the saying of those scholars who do not allow the buyer to demand an oath from the seller, is that an oath is contingent upon a valid claim, and a valid claim is from the opponent. The buyer in this issue can only be an opponent when he proves the defect, i.e., the slave's running away, through legal evidence (shahadah). However, if the buyer could not produce evidence for the defect, then the buyer will not be an opponent, so his claim will not be valid. And when the claim is not valid, an oath will not be contingent upon it. Therefore we said that according to Imam Sahib, the buyer has no right to demand an oath from the seller. And the saying of Sahibayn that 'whatever evidence can be presented for, an oath can also be required for' is incorrect. For example, if someone claims to be a wakil (agent), he will be required to provide evidence for his agency, but if he cannot present evidence for his agency, then the denier of the agency will not be made to take an oath. As for evidence, it does not even require a claim, let alone a valid claim. Rather, it can be established for things where there is no claim at all, such as in hudud: to accept testimony for the hadd of zina, etc., it is not required that a claim be made first; rather, without a claim, if testimony is available according to Shariah, the hadd of zina will be carried out. In contrast, every oath requires a valid claim; without a valid claim, an oath will not be contingent. The difference between oath and evidence is that oath is legislated to end hostility and dispute. Therefore, before an oath is administered, it is necessary that there be an oath? No, the text says 'before an oath is administered, it is necessary that the person to be sworn be an opponent.' And in the mentioned issue, the buyer will be an opponent when he proves the defect, but he has not proved the defect. The conclusion is that in this issue, the buyer did not become an opponent, so his claim will not be valid, and when the buyer's claim is not valid, an oath will not be contingent upon it, meaning the buyer is not allowed to demand an oath from the seller. And evidence is legislated to prove opponent status; thus evidence does not require that the presenter be an opponent before presenting it. However, administering an oath requires that the one administering it be an opponent beforehand. The author of Hidayah says that according to the madhhab of Sahibayn, when the seller refuses to swear an oath of ignorance, then the seller will be made to take a second oath in the manner described at the beginning of the issue. That is, when the seller refuses to swear the oath of ignorance, the defect is proven at the buyer's place due to his refusal, and since the buyer will return the sold item due to this defect, now the seller will swear: 'By Allah, I sold and delivered this slave to you, but this slave never ran away from me,' i.e., this defect never occurred. He will swear with the two methods mentioned in the book that are synonymous with this. If the seller swears that no defect existed on his part, then the buyer will have no option to return the slave, because a new defect (occurring after sale) does not give the buyer the right to return the sold item. However, if the seller also refuses to swear this oath, then the buyer can return the slave, because the defect is proven to have existed with the seller due to the refusal, and a defect that arose with the seller can be grounds for returning the sold item. The author of Hidayah says that if the buyer claims the slave is a runaway in the case of an adult slave, then the seller will swear as follows: 'Since he reached adulthood, this slave has never run away from me,' because if the slave had run away from the seller before adulthood and after adulthood from the buyer, then in that case the buyer would have no right to return the slave, the reason for which has been explained earlier.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding the buyer's right to demand an oath from the seller when a defect (running away) is found in a purchased slave. The discussion begins by stating the procedure for the oath if the buyer cannot produce witnesses. The author then presents the evidence for Imam Abu Hanifa's position that the buyer cannot demand an oath, because an oath is contingent upon a valid claim from an opponent. The buyer becomes an opponent only by proving the defect through evidence. Since he cannot prove it, he is not an opponent, and no oath is required. The author responds to the Sahibayn's argument that wherever evidence can be required, an oath can also be required. He refutes this by giving the example of a person claiming to be an agent (wakil): if he cannot prove agency, the denier is not made to take an oath. Furthermore, evidence can be established without any prior claim, as in hudud cases. The conclusion is that the buyer has no right to demand an oath according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The passage then explains the procedure according to the Sahibayn's madhhab if the seller refuses the first oath (of ignorance). In that case, the seller must take a second oath that the defect did not exist at the time of sale. If he takes this oath, the buyer cannot return the slave; if he refuses, the buyer can return it. Finally, a specific case is mentioned: if the slave is an adult and the buyer claims he ran away after purchase, the seller must swear that the slave did not run away after reaching adulthood, because a pre-adulthood escape is irrelevant.

Key Terms
قسم (qasam): An oath sworn by a person to affirm or deny a claim in a dispute.
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence, usually testimony of two witnesses or equivalent proof.
دعویٰ (da'wa): A claim made by a party in a legal dispute.
خصم (khasm): An opponent in a legal dispute; one who is a party to the conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the condition for a buyer to be considered an opponent (khasm) in a dispute over a defect?
A) The buyer must have paid the full price.
B) The buyer must have a valid claim supported by evidence.
C) The buyer must have returned the item first.
D) The buyer must have witnesses even if they are not credible.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath (qasam) requires a prior valid claim from an opponent, while evidence (bayyinah) can be accepted without any claim—a distinction that affects the buyer's right to demand an oath regarding a defect.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Dispute Over Quantity of Possession: The Buyer's Statement is Accepted
Ruling with proof via qiyas to ghasb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the possessor's statement is accepted in disputes over the quantity of possession, and explain the analogy to ghasb.

Core Idea
In disputes over possession quantity, the possessor's denial of excess is accepted, analogous to ghasb.

When a sale is complete and possession has been taken, disputes can arise about exactly how many items were delivered. The Hanafi school gives great weight to the word of the person who actually possesses the goods. This lesson explores a classic case: the buyer finds a defect and wants to return the item, but the seller claims more was sold. The ruling is clear—the buyer's statement prevails, and the reasoning is grounded in a powerful analogy to the law of usurpation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute Scenario
Read the scenario: buyer purchases a slave girl, takes possession, finds a defect. Seller claims he sold two slave girls; buyer claims only one. Both lack witnesses.
Focus on the key elements: possession taken, defect discovered, and disagreement over quantity.
2
Apply the Principle of Possessor's Statement
The ruling: the buyer's statement is accepted because he is the possessor (qabid) and denies the excess claimed by the seller. The principle is that in disputes over the quantity of possession, the denier-possessor prevails.
The seller is the non-possessor, claiming an excess; the possessor's denial is preferred.
3
Trace the Analogy to Ghasb (Usurpation)
The author supports the ruling by qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) to ghasb: if the usurped-from claims two slaves were usurped and the usurper admits to one, the usurper's statement is accepted. The common illa is that both involve a dispute over the quantity of an item in the possession of the person who holds it.
This analogy shows consistency across different legal contexts.
4
Note the Rule Applies Regardless of Trustee or Liable Status
The text explicitly states that the rule applies whether the possessor is an amin (trustee) or a damin (liable). This emphasizes the strength of the principle.
Even if the possessor is liable for loss (like a buyer who has taken possession), his statement is still accepted.
