پھر توازن برقرار رکھنے کیلئے دوسری جانب میں خود بیٹھ گیا۔ ان صورتوں میں بغیر سوار ہوئے کوئی چارہ کار نہ تھا اسلئے ان صورتوں میں مشتری کا سوار ہونا اس کی رضامندی کی دلیل نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر مشتری کے واسطے سوار نہ ہونے کی گنجائش نکلتی ہو مثلا امور مذکورہ میں سے کوئی بات نہ ہو تو مشتری کا سوار ہونا اس کی رضا مندی قرار دیا جائے گا۔
If testing is done by using it, then riding does not nullify the khiyar al-shart. Explanation: The scenario is that if someone bought a slave-girl and found her wounded, then the buyer treated her, or bought an animal and rode it for his own need, then treating the slave-girl or riding the animal will be considered as acceptance of the defect. Therefore, the buyer will not be permitted to return that slave-girl or riding animal. The proof is that treating the slave-girl or riding the animal for one's own need is evidence that the buyer intended to benefit from it, which only occurs when he intends to keep it. And intending to keep it is only possible when he drops the khiyar al-'ayb and is satisfied with the defect. Thus, it is established that the buyer is satisfied with the defect, and when he is satisfied, his khiyar al-'ayb is also nullified. And when khiyar al-'ayb is nullified, the buyer has no option to return the wounded slave-girl or animal. However, if the riding animal was purchased with a khiyar al-shart and the buyer rode it once, that riding does not nullify the buyer's khiyar al-shart. Because khiyar al-shart is legislated for testing the sold item, and riding once was for testing, since testing can only take place when the sold item is used. Therefore, due to that one ride, the buyer's khiyar al-shart is not nullified. If he rode it to return it to the seller, or to water it, or to buy fodder for it, that is not a sign of acceptance. And if he rode it to return it to its seller, or to water it, or to buy fodder for it, it is not acceptance. As for riding for return, it is a cause of return. And the ruling regarding watering and buying fodder is based on the case when he has no alternative, either because of its difficulty, or his incapability, or because the fodder is in one bundle. But if he has an alternative due to the absence of what we mentioned, then riding will be acceptance. Translation: And if he rode this animal to take it and return it to the seller, or to water it, or to bring fodder for it, then this is not acceptance of the defect. In any case, riding for return is itself a cause of return. And ruling regarding riding for watering or buying fodder is based on the case when the buyer has no alternative, either due to the animal's stubbornness, or due to his incapability, or because the fodder is in one bundle. And if the buyer has an alternative without riding, due to the absence of the mentioned things, then riding will be acceptance. Explanation: The scenario is that if someone purchased a riding animal, then the buyer rode it to return it to the seller, or rode it to water it, or rode it to bring grass for it and load it, then for these reasons, riding will not be called the buyer's acceptance. The author of Al-Hidayah states: If the buyer rode it to return it, then this riding will absolutely not be the buyer's acceptance, whether the buyer has an alternative without riding or not. Because riding to return is a cause of return, and a cause of a thing leads to that thing and strengthens it. So this riding supports return, not acceptance. Therefore, for this purpose, riding will not be considered the buyer's acceptance. And riding for watering or bringing fodder will be considered non-acceptance only when he is compelled to ride, and there is no other possibility, e.g., the animal is stubborn and rebellious, cannot be controlled without riding, or the buyer is extremely weak and old and unable to walk, or there is one bundle of grass placed on one side of the saddle, so he sat on the other side to maintain balance. In these cases, there was no alternative without riding, so in these cases, the buyer's riding is not evidence of his acceptance. And if the buyer had the scope not to ride, e.g., none of the mentioned situations exist, then the buyer's riding will be considered his acceptance.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on when a buyer's actions regarding a purchased item affect his options of khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) and khiyar al-shart (option of condition). The author begins by explaining that if a buyer treats a wounded slave-girl or rides an animal for his own need, this indicates acceptance of the defect and nullifies his right to return the item. The reasoning is that such actions imply an intention to keep the item, which can only happen if the buyer is satisfied with the defect. However, if the animal was bought with a khiyar al-shart (a stipulated option period for testing), riding it once for testing does not nullify that option, because the very purpose of khiyar al-shart is to allow testing by use. The passage then discusses scenarios where riding does not indicate acceptance: if the buyer rides to return the animal to the seller, to water it, or to buy fodder. Riding for return is never acceptance, as it is itself an act of returning. For watering or fodder, it depends on necessity. If the buyer has no alternative due to the animal's stubbornness, his own weakness, or the need to balance a load, then riding is not acceptance. If he could have done it without riding, then riding indicates acceptance and nullifies his khiyar al-'ayb. This distinction preserves the buyer's rights: he is not penalized for necessary actions that do not truly reflect satisfaction with the defect.

Key Terms
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's option to return a defective item and reclaim the full price, provided the defect existed at the time of sale.
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): A stipulated option period in the contract allowing either party to rescind the sale within a specified time, typically for testing the item.
رضا (rida): Acceptance or consent, specifically here the buyer's tacit approval of a defect that nullifies the right to return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases a camel with a khiyar al-shart and rides it once to test its gait. Does this ride nullify his option?
A) Yes, because riding indicates use.
B) No, because khiyar al-shart is for testing.
C) Only if the camel was defective.
D) Yes, but only if the ride exceeded 30 minutes.

Key Takeaway
Riding a purchased animal nullifies khiyar al-'ayb if done for personal benefit, but does not nullify khiyar al-shart if done for testing; riding for return or necessity never indicates acceptance.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Returning a Slave Thief After Punishment: Istihqaq or 'Ayb?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on remedy for buyer of punished slave thief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istihqaq and 'ayb in the context of slave sales, and apply the Sahibayn's ruling to determine the correct compensation.

Core Idea
When a slave's hand is cut off after sale due to prior theft, the Sahibayn treat it as a defect, not total return.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a buyer who unknowingly purchases a slave thief can return the maimed slave for full price or only claim compensation for the defect. The disagreement hinges on whether the amputation is analogous to istihqaq (rightful claim voiding the contract) or 'ayb (defect giving rise to compensation). Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the correct remedy in similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the facts: a slave who had committed theft is sold without the buyer's knowledge, and after the sale the slave's hand is cut off as punishment. The buyer now wants to return the slave.
The punishment occurred in the buyer's possession, but the cause (theft) existed during the seller's possession.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer can return the slave and take the full price. This is because the amputation is equivalent to istihqaq – a right that nullifies the contract from its inception, as if the slave were never validly owned.
Istihqaq usually refers to a third-party claim; here Abu Hanifa extends it to a pre-existing cause of punishment.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that the buyer cannot return the slave but can only recover the difference in value between a thief slave and a non-thief slave. They treat the amputation as a defect ('ayb) in the item.
The contract remains valid, but the item is defective, so the remedy is compensation for the decrease in value, not rescission.
4
Analyze Each Proof
Examine the arguments: The Sahibayn argue that the cause of punishment existed at the seller's hand but does not negate monetary value, so the contract is effective. Abu Hanifa argues that the obligation (wujub) of punishment leads to existence, and existence is attributed to the prior cause, analogous to a stolen slave punished after return to the owner.
Note the analogies: Sahibayn compare to a pregnant slave-girl dying in childbirth (defect), Abu Hanifa compares to a punished usurped slave (istihqaq).
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf and Mufta Bihi
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer returns the maimed slave and gets full price. Under Sahibayn, the buyer keeps the slave and receives only the price difference. The later Hanafi fatwa relies on the Sahibayn's view, as indicated by the text's dismissal of Abu Hanifa's analogy ('the mentioned case is not conceded').
This is a case of legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's position.

Source Text

ایسے غلام کو خریدا جس نے چوری کی تھی مشتری کو علم نہیں تھا پھر غلام کا ہاتھ کاٹا گیا ہمشتری غلام کو واپس کر کے ثمن لے سکتا ہے یا نہیں ، اقوال فقہاء
قال ومن اشترى عبد اقد سرق ولم يعلم به فقطع عند المشترى له ان يرده وياخذ الثمن عند ابي حنيفة وقالا يرجع بما بين قيمة سارقا الى غير سارق وعلى هذا الخلاف اذا قتل بسبب وجدفى يد البائع والحاصل انه بمنزلة الاستحقاق عنده وبمنزلة العيب عندهما لهما ان الموجود فى يد البائع سبب القطع والقتل وانه لا ينافى المالية فنفذ العقد فيه لكنه متعيب فيرجع بنقصانه عند تعذررده وصار كما اذاشترى جارية حاملا فماتت في يده بالولادة فانه يرجع بفضل ما بين قيمتها حاملا الى غير حامل وله ان سبب الوجوب في يد البائع والوجوب يفضى الى الوجود فيكون الجود مضافا الى السبب السابق وصار كما اذا قتل المغصوب اوقطع بعد الرد بجناية وجدت فى يد الغاصب وما ذكر من المسالة ممنوعة ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے ایسا غلام خریدا جس نے چوری کی ہے حالانکہ مشتری کو معلوم نہیں ہے۔ پھر مشتری کے پاس اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا گیا تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک مشتری کو اختیار ہے کہ وہ غلام واپس کر کے پوراشن لے لے۔ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا ہے کہ غلام چور اور غیر چور کی قیمت میں جو فرق ہے وہ لے لے اور اسی اختلاف پر یہ ہے کہ غلام ایسے سب کی وجہ سے قتل کیا گیا جو بائع کے قبضہ میں پایا گیا ہے اور اس اختلاف کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ عیب بمنزلہ استحقاق کے ہے۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک عیب کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بائع کے پاس ہاتھ کاٹے جانے یا قتل کئے جانے کا سبب موجود ہے اور یہ سبب مالیت کے منافی نہیں ہے ۔ پس عقد بیع اس میں نافذ کیا جائے گا لیکن وہ عیب دار ہے تو مشتری مبیع واپس کرنے کے متوزر ہونے کے وقت اپنے بائع سے نقصان عیب واپس لے گا اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کہ اگر کسی نے حاملہ باندی خریدی پھر ولادت کی وجہ سے مشتری کے قبضہ میں مرگئی تو مشتری وہ فاضل قیمت واپس لے گا جو حاملہ اور غیر حاملہ کی قیمتوں کے درمیان ہے اور امام صاحب کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ سزا دا جب ہونے کا سبب بائع کے پاس پایا گیا اور وجوب مفضی الی الوجود ہوتا ہے اسلئے وجود سبب سابق ہی کی طرف منسوب ہوگا اور یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ جب غاصب کے واپس کرنے کے بعد غلام مغصوب کو ایسے جرم کی وجہ سے قتل کیا گیا یا اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا گیا جو جرم غاصب کے قبضہ میں پایا گیا تھا اور مذکورہ مسالہ تسلیم نہیں ہے۔
A slave who had committed theft was bought; the buyer did not know. Then the slave's hand was cut off at the buyer's possession. Can the buyer return the slave and take the price? Statements of the jurists: He said: Whoever buys a slave who had stolen and he did not know, then (the hand) is cut off at the buyer's (possession). For him (the buyer) is to return him and take the price according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He recovers the difference between the value of a thief and a non-thief. And upon this difference (is the case) if he is killed due to a cause found in the seller's hand. The conclusion is that according to him (Abu Hanifa) it is in the status of istihqaq (rightful claim), and according to them (the two) it is in the status of a defect. Their proof is that the cause of cutting and killing existed in the seller's hand, and it does not negate monetary value. So the contract is effective on it, but it is defective. So he recovers the decrease when returning it is difficult (i.e., the item is lost or damaged). And it becomes like if someone bought a pregnant slave-girl who died in his hand due to childbirth, then he recovers the excess between her value as pregnant and as non-pregnant. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that the cause of wajub (obligation of punishment) existed in the seller's hand, and obligation leads to existence, so the existence is attributed to the prior cause. And it becomes like when a stolen (slave) is killed or cut after return due to a crime found in the hand of the usurper. And the mentioned case is not conceded. Translation: And if someone bought a slave who had stolen, while the buyer did not know. Then at the buyer's possession his hand was cut off. So according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer has the option to return the slave and take the full price. And the two Sahibayn said that he takes the difference between the price of a thief slave and a non-thief slave. And upon this same difference is the case if the slave was killed due to a cause found in the seller's possession. And the outcome of this difference is that according to Imam Abu Hanifa this is in the status of istihqaq (total right to return), and according to the two it is in the status of a defect. The proof of the two is that the cause of hand cutting or killing existed in the seller's hand, and this cause does not negate monetary value. So the contract of sale is executed on him, but he is defective. So the buyer, when it becomes difficult to return the sold item, will recover the loss of defect from his seller. And this is like if someone bought a pregnant slave-girl who died in his hand due to childbirth, then the buyer recovers the excess value between pregnant and non-pregnant. And Imam's (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that the cause of wajub (obligation of punishment) existed in the seller's hand, and obligation leads to existence, so existence is attributed to the prior cause. And this is like when a stolen (slave) after return is killed or cut due to a crime found in the usurper's hand. And the mentioned case is not conceded.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a buyer who unknowingly purchases a slave who had committed theft. After the sale, the slave's hand is cut off as a prescribed punishment for theft. The central question is whether the buyer can return the slave to the seller and reclaim the full price. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer may return the slave and take full price because the defect (the hand amputation) is equivalent to istihqaq (complete right of return) since the cause of the amputation existed in the seller's hand. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) view this as a defect (not total right of return), so the buyer can only claim the difference in value between a thief slave and a non-thief slave. Their reasoning: the cause (the theft) existed at the seller's end, and the punishment does not negate the item's inherent property value; the contract remains valid but the item is defective, so compensation is for the defect. Abu Hanifa analogizes to a case where a stolen slave is returned and then punished due to a crime committed during the usurper's possession; the punishment is attributed to the prior cause. The Sahibayn analogize to a pregnant slave-girl dying in childbirth; the buyer claims only the difference in value due to the pregnancy (a similar defect). This dispute shows how different categorizations (istihqaq vs. 'ayb) lead to different remedies: full return vs. compensation for decrease.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): A legal claim or entitlement; here, it means the buyer has the right to rescind the contract entirely, as if the slave were not his property due to a pre-existing cause.
عیب ('ayb): A defect in the sold item that existed at the time of sale, giving the buyer the option to either return the item or claim compensation (arsh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in the case of a slave thief whose hand is cut off after sale?
A) Abu Hanifa allows full return; Sahibayn allow only compensation for the price difference.
B) Abu Hanifa allows compensation; Sahibayn allow full return.
C) Both allow full return.
D) Both allow only compensation.

Key Takeaway
When a pre-existing cause results in a post-sale legal punishment, the Sahibayn treat it as a defect; the buyer cannot return but claims compensation for the decrease in value.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Buyer's Recourse for Slave with Pre-Sale Theft or Murder
Intra-Hanafi dispute on buyer's remedy for pre-sale offense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on a buyer's remedy when a purchased slave is punished for a pre-sale offense, and identify the legal reasoning (qiyas) that distinguishes the positions.

Core Idea
The buyer's remedy depends on whether the pre-sale offense is treated as a total void (istihqaq) or a hidden defect ('aib) — a classic Hanafi usul dispute between qiyas and istihsan.

When a slave commits an offense before sale and is punished afterward, the buyer's entitlement to recourse becomes a matter of legal characterization. Imam Abu Hanifa and his foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree on whether the situation resembles a third-party claim (istihqaq) or a pre-existing defect ('aib). This lesson unpacks their arguments, analogies, and the practical consequences for the buyer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two positions
Read the scenario: a slave committed theft or murder while with the seller, the buyer was unaware, and after possession the slave's hand is cut or he is executed. State Abu Hanifa's remedy (return and full price or keep and half-price) and the Sahibayn's remedy (only difference in value).
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; both positions are within the school.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's qiyas on istihqaq
Abu Hanifa treats the situation as analogous to a third-party claim (istihqaq). The cause of punishment existed with the seller, so the punishment is attributed to the seller's period, voiding the sale as if the slave was never the seller's property. The analogy to a misappropriated slave punished after return supports full recovery.
The cause (sabab) leads to the effect (musabbab), so the seller bears the loss.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's qiyas on 'aib
The Sahibayn treat the pre-sale offense as a hidden defect ('aib). The contract is valid because the cause does not negate marketability; the slave is simply defective. Since return is impossible (hand cut or slave dead), the buyer recovers only the depreciation in value, analogous to a pregnant slave who dies in childbirth.
The defect is pre-existing, but the sale is not void; compensation is limited to arsh al-'aib.
4
Analyze the counter-arguments and thamarah al-khilaf
Abu Hanifa rejects the pregnancy analogy because the defect there is inherent, while here the punishment is a legal consequence that retrospectively destroys value. The practical difference (thamarah) is significant: full price vs. deficiency only.
The thamarah illustrates how usul principles affect real transactions.
5
Note the tarjih (or lack thereof) in this passage
The author of Hidayah (al-Marghinani) does not give preference; he merely presents the divergence. In later Hanafi practice, the Sahibayn's view is often relied upon because it preserves the contract except for the defect, but both views are considered valid.
Knowing that tarjih is not given here encourages the student to weigh the proofs independently.

Source Text

تشریح: صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے ایسا غلام خریدا جس نے بائع کے پاس رہتے ہوئے چوری کی ہے اور مشتری کو اس کا علم نہ خریدتے وقت ہوا اور نہ قبضہ کرتے وقت ہوا۔ پھر اس غلام کا ہاتھ مشتری کے قبضہ میں کیونکہ آدمی کا ایک ہاتھ آدھے شمن کے برابر ہوتا ہے۔ صاحبین نے فرمایا ہے کہ چور اور نجیمی چور کی قیمتوں میں جو تفاوت ہو گا وہ لے لے۔ مثلاً چور کی قیمت ایک ہزار روپے ہے اور غیر چور کی قیمت پندرہ سو روپے قبضہ میں قتل کیا گیا تو حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک مشتری بائع سے پور امن واپس لے گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک ایسے غلام کی قیمت معلوم کی جائے گی جو واجب القتل اور مباح الدم ہے اور ایسے غلام کی قیمت معلوم کی جائے گی جو واجب القتل نہیں ہے بلکہ معصوم الدم ہے۔ ان دونوں کے درمیان قیمت کا جو تفاوت آئے گا۔ مشتری بائع سے اس کو واپس لے سکتا ہے مثلاً مباح الدم اور واجب القتل غلام کی قیمت دو سو روپیہ ہے اور معصوم الدم او غیر واجب القتل کی قیمت ایک ہزار روپیہ ہے تو مشتری اپنے بائع سے آٹھ سو روپیہ واپس لے گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس اختلاف کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ غلام کا مباح الید یا مباح الدم ہونا حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک بمنزلہ استحقاق کے ہے اور مستحق کے ظاہر ہونے کے بعد چونکہ مشتری پوراثمن واپس لینے کا مجاز ہو گا اور صاحبین کے نزدیک بمنزلہ عیب کے ہے اور مبیع اگر عیب دار ہو اور اس کا واپس کرنا مجود رہو گیا ہوتو مشتری بائع سے نقصان عیب لینے کا اختیار رکھتا ہے اور یہاں بھی غلام کا مباح الید یا مباح الدم ہونا تو عیب ہے اور اس کی واپسی کا حعذر ہو نا قتل کی صورت میں تو ظاہر ہے کیونکہ جب قصاص یا مرتد ہونے کی وجہ سے غلام مشتری کے پاس قتل کر دیا گیا تو اس کو واپس کرنا متعد راور ناممکن ہو گیا ہے اور قطع ید کی صورت میں اس لئے کہ غلام کا ہاتھ مشتری کے قبضہ میں کاٹا گیا ہے حالانکہ اسکا وجوب مشتری کے قبضہ میں نہیں ہوا تھا پس ہاتھ کا کٹنا ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ مشتری کے پاس کوئی عیب جدید پیدا ہو گیا ہو اور مشتری کے پاس عیب جدید کا پیدا ہونا عیب سابق کی وجہ سے مشتری کیلئے مانع ہوتا ہے۔ پس جب غلام کا واپس کرنا مستعد رہو گیا حالانکہ غلام بائع کے پاس سے عیب دار آیا ہے تو مشتری کے درمیان جو تفاوت آئے گا اس کو بائع سے واپس لے لے گا۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ بائع کے پاس ہاتھ کاٹے جانے یا قتل کئے جانے کا سبب موجود ہے اور اس سبب سے یہ لازم نہیں آتا کہ غلام کی مالیت نہ رہے چنانچہ اس کی بیع جائز ہے پس عقد بھی نافذ ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن وہ غلام مباح الید یا مباح الدم ہونے کی وجہ سے عیب دار ہے اور مشتری کیلئے اس عیب دار غلام کا واپس کمرنا متعذر ہو گا ہے جیسا کہ توضیح مسئلہ کے ذیل میں گذر چکا ہے اور جس صورت میں مبیع معیب ہو اور اس کا واپس کرنا مستعد رہو تو مشتری نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا مجاز ہوتا ہے اسلئے اس صورت میں بھی اس کو نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ اور یہ ایسا ہے جیسا کہ کسی نے حاملہ باندی خریدی اور اس کو حمل کا علم نہ خریدتے وقت ہوسکا اور نہ قبضہ کرتے وقت ہو سکا پھر وہ ولادت کی وجہ سے مشتری کے قبضہ میں مرگئی تو مشتری حاملہ باندی اور غیر حاملہ باندی کی قیمت کا اندازہ کر کے دونوں کی قیمتوں کے درمیان جو فرق ہے اس کو واپس لیتا ہے اس کو نقصان عیب کہتے ہیں پس اسی طرح مشتری مذکورہ مسئلہ میں بائع سے نقصان
He said: Whoever buys a slave who had stolen and does not know of it, and then the hand is cut off while in the buyer's possession — according to Abu Hanifa, he has the option to return him and take the full price. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He recovers only the difference between the value of a thief and a non-thief. The same disagreement applies if he is killed due to a cause found in the seller's possession. The upshot: In his (Abu Hanifa's) view, it is like a claim of entitlement (istihqaq); in their view, it is like a defect. Their proof: The cause of cutting or killing existed in the seller's possession, and this does not negate marketability, so the contract is valid, but the slave is defective, so he recovers the deficiency when return is impossible. It is like buying a pregnant slave girl who dies in his possession due to childbirth: he recovers the difference between her value pregnant and non-pregnant. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof: The cause of the obligation existed in the seller's possession, and obligation leads to existence, so the actual occurrence is attributed to the prior cause. It is like when a misappropriated slave is killed or mutilated after return due to an offense found while in the usurper's possession. The mentioned case is disputed.

Explanation: The scenario is: Someone buys a slave who had committed theft while with the seller, and the buyer did not know at the time of purchase or taking possession. Then the slave's hand is cut off in the buyer's possession. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer has the option to either return the handless slave and take full price, or keep the slave and take half the price because a hand is worth half the body. The two disciples said: He takes only the difference in value between a thief and a non-thief. For example, if a thief slave is worth 1000 rupees and a non-thief is worth 1500, the buyer takes 500 rupees from the seller, considering the theft caused a decrease in value. The same disagreement applies if the slave is killed in the buyer's possession due to a cause that occurred with the seller, such as murder or apostasy. According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer takes full price; according to them, he takes only the difference between the value of a slave who is liable for execution (mubah al-dam) and one who is not. The author of Hidayah says the essence is that Abu Hanifa treats the loss as total (istihqaq), while they treat it as a defect. So when return is impossible (due to death or new defect), the buyer recovers only the deficiency. Their proof: The cause existed with the seller, but it does not destroy the slave's marketability; the contract is valid, but the slave is defective. Since return is impossible (as the hand was cut after purchase or the slave died), the buyer takes the defect compensation. It is like the pregnant slave example. Abu Hanifa's proof: The cause existed with the seller, so the effect is attributed to that cause, making it as if the slave was lost due to the seller's cause, entitling full return.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a buyer's remedy when a purchased slave is later punished (hand cut or execution) for an offense committed before the sale, unbeknownst to the buyer. The ruling belongs to the chapter of sales (buyu'), specifically regarding defects and options. The disagreement is between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Abu Hanifa analogizes the situation to istihqaq (a claim of ownership by a third party), where the buyer is entitled to return the slave and recover the full price because the punishment effectively nullifies the subject matter of the sale. The Sahibayn analogize it to a pre-existing defect ('aib) that becomes apparent after purchase; since the contract was valid and the cause existed with the seller, the buyer cannot return the slave (as the hand was cut or the slave executed after delivery), but can claim arsh al-'aib (compensation for the defect, i.e., the difference in value between a sound and defective slave). The author of the commentary explains the two positions and their respective qiyas (analogies). The Sahibayn support their view by comparing to a pregnant slave who dies in childbirth after purchase: the buyer cannot return her but recovers the depreciation in value due to the hidden defect (pregnancy). Abu Hanifa supports his by comparing to a misappropriated slave who, after return to the owner, is punished for an offense committed while in the usurper's hands: in that case, the owner can claim the full value from the usurper, so here the seller should bear the full loss. This is a classic example of istihsan (juristic preference) vs. qiyas. The passage is an explanatory expansion of the Hidayah's matn.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): In this passage: a claim of entitlement by a third party that makes the slave not fully owned by the seller; it voids the sale retroactively.
عيب ('aib): A defect in the slave that existed at the time of sale but was unknown to the buyer; it entitles the buyer to compensation but not full return if return is impossible.
مباح الدم (mubāh al-dam): One whose blood is lawful to shed, i.e., liable to execution (e.g., murderer, apostate). The slave's value is diminished because he is subject to capital punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if a purchased slave had committed theft before the sale and his hand is cut off after the buyer takes possession, what is the buyer's remedy?
A) Return the slave and recover the full price
B) Keep the slave and recover half the price
C) Recover the difference in value between a thief and a non-thief slave
D) No recourse because the buyer assumed the risk

Key Takeaway
The buyer's remedy for a pre-sale offense hinges on whether the offense is treated as a total void (istihqaq) or a hidden defect ('aib) — a distinction that leads to markedly different outcomes in the Hanafi school.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Buyer's Recourse for a Slave Punished for a Pre-Purchase Offense
Explains the Hanafi dispute on buyer's recourse when a slave is punished for an offense committed with the seller.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the buyer's recourse when a purchased slave is punished for a pre-existing cause, and apply the underlying legal reasoning to analogous cases.

Core Idea
A slave punished for a cause existing with the seller is either an entitlement voiding the sale or a defect entitling compensation.

When a slave is punished for an offense committed before the sale, the buyer seeks recourse from the seller. The Hanafi school is divided: Imam Abu Hanifa treats the pre-existing liability as a third-party entitlement, allowing the buyer to reclaim the full price. The Sahibayn, however, consider it a defect that existed at sale, and since return is impossible after punishment, the buyer may only claim the depreciation in value. This lesson unpacks the proofs and analogies behind each view, including the famous cases of hand-cutting, execution, and even double theft. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Hanafi sales law (buyuʿ).

Guided Path
1
State the rulings
State the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn for the basic case: hand cutting and execution.
Imam: full or half price; Sahibayn: defect compensation.
2
Trace the proofs
For each view, identify the analogy and the underlying illa (cause).
Imam: usurper analogy; Sahibayn: pregnant slave analogy.
3
Examine the double theft scenario
Apply the same principles when the slave steals both before and after purchase, resulting in a single hand cut.
Imam: quarter/three-quarters; Sahibayn: defect compensation.
4
Evaluate the practical difference
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf: what the buyer actually recovers under each view.
Imam: larger recovery; Sahibayn: smaller recovery.

کے قبضہ میں مرگئی تو مشتری حاملہ باندی اور غیر حاملہ باندی کی قیمت کا اندازہ کر کے دونوں کی قیمتوں کے درمیان جو فرق ہے اس کو واپس لیتا ہے اس کو نقصان عیب کہتے ہیں پس اسی طرح مشتری مذکورہ مسئلہ میں بائع سے نقصان
حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام کا ہاتھ کاٹے جانے یا اس کو قتل کئے جانے کا سبب بائع کے قبضہ میں پایا گیا ہے اور و جوب کا سبب مفضی الی الوجوب ہوتا ہے تو گویا ہاتھ کتنا یا قتل کیا جانا بائع ہی کے پاس واجب ہوا ہے اور وجوب مفضی الی الوجود ہوتا ہے تو گویا غلام کے ہاتھ کا کٹنا یا اسکا قتل کیا جانا سبب سابق کی وجہ سے بائع ہی کے قبضہ میں موجود ہوا ہے۔ پس جب ہاتھ کا کٹنا یا غلام کا قتل کیا جانا اس سبب سے موجود ہوا جو بائع کے قبضہ میں تھا تو یہ غلام مستحق کے مانند ہو گیا یعنی یہ غلام بائع کا تھا ہی نہیں بلکہ اس پر دوسرے کا استحقاق تھا جس کا ظہو ر اب ہوا ہے اور مستحق کو عقد بیع شامل نہیں ہوتا اور جب مستحق کو عقد بیع شامل نہیں ہوتا تو دوسرے سے مشتری کا قبضہ ہی نہیں ہوا اور جب مشتری کا قبضہ ثابت نہیں ہوا تو وہ اپنا دیا ہواشمن بائع سے واپس لے لے اور ہاتھ کٹنے کی صورت میں چونکہ آدھا غلام مستحق ہوا ہے، اسلئے مشتری آدھا من واپس لے گا ۔ یا مقطوع الید غلام کو واپس کر کے پوراثمن واپس لے گا۔ اس کی مثال ایسی ہے جیسے غاصب کے پاس غلام نے ایسی حرکت کی جس سے اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا جانا یا قتل کیا جانا لازم ہے لیکن غاصب نے جس سے غصب کیا تھا اس کو واپس کر دیا اور یہاں اس کا ہاتھ کانٹا گیا یا قتل کیا گیا تو مالک یعنی مغصوب منہ غاصب سے غلام کی پوری قیمت لیتا ہے۔ بشرطیکہ اس کو قتل کیا گیا ہو جیسا کہ غاصب کے قبضہ میں قتل کئے جانے کی صورت میں مالک غاصب سے پوری قیمت لیتا ہے اور ہاتھ کاٹے جانے کی صورت میں مالک غاصب سے آدھی قیمت لیتا ہے یا ہاتھ کٹا غلام غاصب کو واپس کر کے پوری قیمت اس سے وصول کرے گا۔ اسی طرح مسئلہ مذکورہ میں اگر غلام مشتری کے قبضہ میں قصاص یا ردت کی وجہ سے قتل کیا گیا تو مشتری بائع سے پوراشن لے گا اور اگر اس کا ہاتھ کا نا گیا تو مشتری بائع سے آدھاشن لے یا اس کو واپس کر کے پوراشن لے لے۔
اور حاملہ باندی کے حق میں جو حکم ذکر کیا گیا ہے وہ صاحبین کا مذہب ہے ورنہ حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک حکم یہ ہے کہ جب حاملہ باندی ولادت کی وجہ سے مرگئی تو مشتری بائع سے پوراشن لے گا۔ اور جب امام صاحب کا مذہب یہ ہے تو حاملہ باندی کے مسئلہ کو امام صاحب کے خلاف بطور قیاس پیش کرنا کس طرح درست ہو گا۔
غلام نے بائع کے پاس چوری کی پھر مشتری کے ہاتھ میں چوری کی دونوں چوریوں سے ہاتھ کاٹا گیا مشتری نقصان عیب کے ساتھ رجوع کر سکتا ہے یا نہیں، اقوال فقہاء
ولو سرق فى يد البائع ثم فى يد المشترى فقطع بهما عندهما يرجع بالنقصان كما ذكرنا وعنده لايرده بدون رضاء البائع للعيب الحادث ويرجع بربع الثمن وان قبله البائع فبثلثة ارباع لان اليد من الادمى نصفه وقد تلفت بالجنايتين وفى احدهما الرجوع فيتنصف
ترجمہ اور اگر غلام نے بائع کے قبضہ میں چوری کی پھر مشتری کے پاس چوری کی پھر دونوں چوریوں کی وجہ سے اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا گیا تو صاحبین کے نزدیک مشتری نقصان عیب واپس لے گا۔ جیسے ہم نے ذکر کیا ہے اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک نیا عیب پیدا ہونے کی وجہ سے بغیر بائع کی رضا مندی کے اس کو واپس نہیں کر سکتا ہے اور چوتھائی ثمن واپس لے گا اور اگر بائع نے ہاتھ کٹا ہوا غلام قبول کر لیا تو مشتری تین چوتھائی ثمن واپس لے گا کیونکہ آدمی کا ہاتھ اس کا آدھا ہوتا ہے اور وہ دو جرموں کی وجہ سے تلف ہوا اور ان دونوں میں سے ایک میں
Explanation of the case: If someone buys a slave who committed theft while with the seller, and the buyer did not know of it at the time of purchase or possession, then the slave's hand is cut off while in the buyer's possession. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer has the option: either return the hand-cut slave and take back the full price from the seller, or keep the hand-cut slave and take half the price from the seller, because a person's hand is half of his value. The Sahibayn said: the buyer takes the difference between the value of a thief and a non-thief. For example, if the thief slave's value is 1000 rupees and the non-thief slave's value is 1500 rupees, the buyer takes back 500 rupees from the seller, and it is understood that the value decreased by 500 rupees due to the theft. This same difference between Imam and the Sahibayn exists when the slave is killed while in the buyer's possession due to a cause that occurred with the seller. For example, if while with the seller the slave intentionally killed someone or apostatized, and then the slave is executed by retribution or for apostasy while in the buyer's possession, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa the buyer takes back the full price from the seller, while according to the Sahibayn the value of a slave who is liable for execution and whose blood is permissible (mubah al-dam) is determined, and the value of a slave who is not liable for execution and whose blood is inviolable is determined; the difference between these two values the buyer can take back from the seller. For example, if the value of a slave whose blood is permissible and liable for execution is 200 rupees, and the value of a slave whose blood is inviolable and not liable for execution is 1000 rupees, then the buyer takes back 800 rupees from his seller. The author of al-Hidayah says: The gist of this difference is that the slave's being mubah al-yad or mubah al-dam is, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, like entitlement (istihqaq), and after the entitled person appears, the buyer is entitled to take back the full price. According to the Sahibayn, it is like a defect, and if the sold item is defective and its return is impossible, the buyer has the right to claim compensation for the defect from the seller. Here too the slave's being mubah al-yad or mubah al-dam is a defect, and its return is impossible — in the case of execution it is obvious because when the slave is executed by retribution or apostasy while with the buyer, returning him becomes difficult and impossible; and in the case of hand-cutting because the slave's hand was cut in the buyer's possession while its obligation did not occur in the buyer's possession. Thus the cutting of the hand becomes like a new defect arising with the buyer, and the occurrence of a new defect with the buyer prevents the buyer from returning the slave due to the pre-existing defect. So when returning the slave becomes impossible even though the slave came defective from the seller, the buyer takes back from the seller the difference that arose. The Sahibayn's proof: the cause of the hand-cutting or execution existed with the seller, but this cause does not necessitate that the slave's value is nullified; thus his sale is valid, and the contract is effective. However, the slave is defective due to being mubah al-yad or mubah al-dam, and returning this defective slave is impossible for the buyer, as has passed in the explanation. And when the sold item is defective and its return is impossible, the buyer is entitled to claim compensation for the defect; thus in this case he will have the option to claim compensation for the defect. This is like if someone buys a pregnant slave girl and does not know of the pregnancy at purchase or possession, then she dies due to childbirth while in the buyer's possession; the buyer estimates the value of a pregnant slave girl and a non-pregnant one and takes back the difference between the two values; this is called compensation for defect. So likewise in the mentioned case the buyer will take compensation for defect from the seller.
Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: the cause of the slave's hand being cut or being executed existed with the seller, and the cause of obligation leads to the obligation, so the cutting of the hand or execution became obligatory while with the seller. And obligation leads to existence, so the cutting of the hand or execution existed with the seller due to the prior cause. Since the cutting or execution occurred due to a cause that was with the seller, this slave became like an entitled one (mustahaqq), i.e., this slave was not the seller's at all, but rather there was another's entitlement to him, which has now appeared. The contract of sale does not include the entitled object, and when the contract does not include it, the buyer's possession from the seller is not established. Since the buyer's possession is not established, he takes back the price he paid. In the case of hand-cutting, since half the slave became entitled, the buyer takes back half the price, or returns the hand-cut slave and takes back the full price. An example: if with a usurper the slave does an act that necessitates cutting his hand or execution, but the usurper returns him to the owner, and here his hand is cut or he is executed, then the owner (the one from whom it was usurped) takes the full value from the usurper. If executed while in the usurper's possession, the owner takes the full value; if the hand is cut, the owner takes half the value, or returns the hand-cut slave to the usurper and receives the full value. Similarly in the mentioned case: if the slave is executed by retribution or apostasy while in the buyer's possession, the buyer takes the full price from the seller; if his hand is cut, the buyer takes half the price or returns him and takes the full price.
As for the ruling mentioned regarding the pregnant slave girl, that is the opinion of the Sahibayn; according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the ruling is that when the pregnant slave girl dies due to childbirth, the buyer takes back the full price from the seller. And since Imam's opinion is this, how can the case of the pregnant slave girl be presented as an analogy against Imam?
The slave committed theft while with the seller, then committed theft while with the buyer, and his hand was cut for both thefts. Can the buyer seek recourse for defect compensation? The sayings of the jurists:
"And if he stole while in the hand of the seller then while in the hand of the buyer, and it was cut for both, according to them (Sahibayn) he takes recourse for the difference as we mentioned, and according to him (Abu Hanifa) he does not return it without the seller's consent due to the new defect, and he takes back a quarter of the price; and if the seller accepts it, then three-quarters, because the hand of a human is half of him, and it was destroyed due to two offenses, and in one of them there is recourse, so it is halved."
Translation: And if the slave stole while in the seller's possession, then stole while in the buyer's possession, then his hand was cut for both thefts, then according to the Sahibayn the buyer takes back the defect compensation as we mentioned. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, due to the new defect, he cannot return it without the seller's consent, and he takes back a quarter of the price; and if the seller accepts the hand-cut slave, the buyer takes back three-quarters of the price, because a person's hand is half of him, and it perished due to two crimes, and in one of them there is recourse, so it is halved.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the buyer's recourse when a purchased slave is punished (hand cut or execution) for an offense committed prior to the sale (while with the seller). The core issue is the legal characterization of the slave's liability. Imam Abu Hanifa considers it tantamount to a third-party entitlement (istihqaq): since the cause for the punishment existed with the seller, the slave is as if he was never validly owned by the seller, and the buyer's possession is not established. Therefore the buyer may reclaim the full price, or in hand-cutting cases half the price (since a hand is half the value). The Sahibayn view it as a defect (ʿayb) that existed at the time of sale but became impossible to return due to a new event (the punishment). Consequently, the buyer may only claim compensation for the difference in value between a sound slave and a defective one. The text elaborates both proofs: the Sahibayn reason by analogy to a pregnant slave who dies in childbirth, where the buyer receives compensation for the defect of pregnancy. Imam Abu Hanifa analogizes to a usurper's liability when the slave is punished in his possession. A further scenario is discussed where the slave steals both before and after purchase, leading to a single hand cut for two thefts. Here, the Sahibayn still apply defect compensation, while Imam Abu Hanifa rules that the buyer cannot return the slave due to a new defect and recovers only a quarter of the price (half of half, since the hand perished by two crimes). If the seller accepts the slave, the buyer recovers three-quarters. This lesson demonstrates the application of legal reasoning (talil) in Hanafi jurisprudence, showing how different starting assumptions lead to different practical outcomes.

Key Terms
مقطوع الید (maqṭūʿ al-yad): A slave whose hand has been cut off as a legal punishment (ḥadd) for theft.
مباح الید (mubāḥ al-yad): A slave whose hand is lawfully liable to be cut off (i.e., the punishment is applicable).
مباح الدم (mubāḥ al-dam): A slave whose life is lawfully forfeit (e.g., due to apostasy or murder).
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Entitlement of a third party to the slave, making the sale as if it never included that slave.
عیب (ʿayb): A defect in the sold item that existed at the time of sale, giving the buyer a right of return or compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the buyer entitled to if a slave's hand is cut off for a theft committed while with the seller?
A) The difference in value between a thief and a non-thief slave.
B) The full price by returning the slave, or half the price by keeping him.
C) A quarter of the price only.
D) Nothing, because the sale is valid and the punishment is after possession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sales law, the characterization of a pre-existing liability as either entitlement or defect determines whether the buyer recovers the full price or only depreciation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Ruling on a Slave Killed or Maimed Due to Cause in Seller's Possession
Explains Imam Abu Hanifa's reasoning for buyer's right to reclaim price when slave lost due to seller-side cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling when a sold slave is lost due to a pre-existing cause in the seller's possession and apply the analogy to the usurper case.

Core Idea
A cause of loss in the seller's possession makes the slave like a claimed item, entitling the buyer to reclaim the price.

This lesson examines Imam Abu Hanifa's reasoning for holding the seller liable when a slave is killed or maimed due to a cause that existed while the slave was with the seller. The argument draws an analogy (qiyas) to a usurper's liability, establishing that the buyer should not bear the loss. We will also see an intra-Hanafi dispute with the Sahibayn regarding a pregnant slave-girl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling scenario
State the case: a slave is sold, then in the buyer's possession is killed (by qisas or apostasy) or has a hand cut off due to an act committed while in the seller's possession.
2
Understand the legal reasoning
Imam Abu Hanifa argues that because the cause of the punishment (the act leading to death/maiming) occurred in the seller's possession, the loss is attributed to the seller. The slave is treated as if never validly sold, like a claimed property (mustahaq).
The seller's possession of the cause is key, not negligence.
3
Trace the analogy to the usurper case
The text compares the seller to a usurper (ghasib): if a usurper's slave is killed due to an act done while with the usurper, the owner recovers full value from the usurper. Similarly, the buyer recovers from the seller.
This is qiyas khafi supported by istihsan.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
On the pregnant slave-girl case, the Sahibayn differ. Abu Hanifa holds that the pregnancy (cause of death) was present with the seller, so buyer takes full price. The Sahibayn's view is not detailed but implied to be different.
The text criticizes presenting this as an analogy against Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام کا ہاتھ کاٹے جانے یا اس کو قتل کئے جانے کا سبب بائع کے قبضہ میں پایا گیا ہے اور و جوب کا سبب مفضی الی الوجوب ہوتا ہے تو گویا ہاتھ کتنا یا قتل کیا جانا بائع ہی کے پاس واجب ہوا ہے اور وجوب مفضی الی الوجود ہوتا ہے تو گویا غلام کے ہاتھ کا کٹنا یا اسکا قتل کیا جانا سبب سابق کی وجہ سے بائع ہی کے قبضہ میں موجود ہوا ہے۔ پس جب ہاتھ کا کٹنا یا غلام کا قتل کیا جانا اس سبب سے موجود ہوا جو بائع کے قبضہ میں تھا تو یہ غلام مستحق کے مانند ہو گیا یعنی یہ غلام بائع کا تھا ہی نہیں بلکہ اس پر دوسرے کا استحقاق تھا جس کا ظہو ر اب ہوا ہے اور مستحق کو عقد بیع شامل نہیں ہوتا اور جب مستحق کو عقد بیع شامل نہیں ہوتا تو دوسرے سے مشتری کا قبضہ ہی نہیں ہوا اور جب مشتری کا قبضہ ثابت نہیں ہوا تو وہ اپنا دیا ہواشمن بائع سے واپس لے لے اور ہاتھ کٹنے کی صورت میں چونکہ آدھا غلام مستحق ہوا ہے، اسلئے مشتری آدھا من واپس لے گا ۔ یا مقطوع الید غلام کو واپس کر کے پوراثمن واپس لے گا۔ اس کی مثال ایسی ہے جیسے غاصب کے پاس غلام نے ایسی حرکت کی جس سے اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا جانا یا قتل کیا جانا لازم ہے لیکن غاصب نے جس سے غصب کیا تھا اس کو واپس کر دیا اور یہاں اس کا ہاتھ کانٹا گیا یا قتل کیا گیا تو مالک یعنی مغصوب منہ غاصب سے غلام کی پوری قیمت لیتا ہے۔ بشرطیکہ اس کو قتل کیا گیا ہو جیسا کہ غاصب کے قبضہ میں قتل کئے جانے کی صورت میں مالک غاصب سے پوری قیمت لیتا ہے اور ہاتھ کاٹے جانے کی صورت میں مالک غاصب سے آدھی قیمت لیتا ہے یا ہاتھ کٹا غلام غاصب کو واپس کر کے پوری قیمت اس سے وصول کرے گا۔ اسی طرح مسئلہ مذکورہ میں اگر غلام مشتری کے قبضہ میں قصاص یا ردت کی وجہ سے قتل کیا گیا تو مشتری بائع سے پوراشن لے گا اور اگر اس کا ہاتھ کا نا گیا تو مشتری بائع سے آدھاشن لے یا اس کو واپس کر کے پوراشن لے لے۔
اور حاملہ باندی کے حق میں جو حکم ذکر کیا گیا ہے وہ صاحبین کا مذہب ہے ورنہ حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک حکم یہ ہے کہ جب حاملہ باندی ولادت کی وجہ سے مرگئی تو مشتری بائع سے پوراشن لے گا۔ اور جب امام صاحب کا مذہب یہ ہے تو حاملہ باندی کے مسئلہ کو امام صاحب کے خلاف بطور قیاس پیش کرنا کس طرح درست ہو گا۔
The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the cause of the slave's hand being cut off or being killed was found in the seller's possession, and a cause of obligation leads to obligation, so it is as if the cutting or killing became obligatory upon the seller, and obligation leads to existence, so it is as if the cutting or killing occurred in the seller's possession due to the prior cause. Therefore, when the cutting or killing occurred due to a cause in the seller's possession, this slave becomes like one who is claimed (mustahaq), meaning this slave did not actually belong to the seller but rather there was a claim of another which has now appeared, and the contract of sale does not include the claimed property. Since the contract does not include it, the buyer did not take possession from the seller, and since the buyer's possession is not established, he takes back his price from the seller. In the case of the hand being cut off, since half of the slave is claimed, the buyer takes back half the price, or returns the hand-cut slave and takes the full price. Its example is like a slave under a usurper who performs an act necessitating his hand being cut off or being killed, but the usurper returns him to the owner, and then the hand is cut off or he is killed; in that case, the owner (the one from whom it was usurped) takes the full value from the usurper if killed, or half if the hand is cut off, or returns the hand-cut slave and takes full value. Similarly, in the mentioned issue, if the slave is killed under the buyer's possession due to qisas or apostasy, the buyer takes the full price from the seller; if his hand is cut off, the buyer takes half the price or returns him and takes full price. As for the pregnant slave-girl, the ruling mentioned is the school of the Sahibayn; according to Imam Abu Hanifa, when a pregnant slave-girl dies due to childbirth, the buyer takes the full price from the seller. And when Imam's school is so, how can the case of the pregnant slave be presented as an analogy against Imam?
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Abu Hanifa's evidentiary reasoning for the ruling on a slave who is killed or loses a hand due to a cause that occurred while in the seller's possession. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute, as it contrasts Imam Abu Hanifa's position with that of the Sahibayn regarding the pregnant slave case. The reasoning employs a qiyas khafi (istihsan) analogy to the case of a usurper (ghasib). The key principle is that if the cause of the loss (such as an act requiring retaliation) existed in the seller's possession, the loss is attributed to the seller, making the slave as if he was never validly sold. The buyer can reclaim the price accordingly. The pregnant slave case is an exception: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer takes full price if the slave dies due to childbirth, while the Sahibayn hold a different view.

Key Terms
بائع (bā'iʿ): The seller in a sale contract.
مشتری (mushtarī): The buyer in a sale contract.
غاصب (ghāṣib): One who wrongfully takes possession of another's property (usurper).
مستحق (mustaḥaqq): A rightful claimant; a third party who has a valid claim of ownership over the property.
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment for intentional homicide or injury.
ردت (riddah): Apostasy from Islam; punishable by death in classical Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Return the slave and take full price
B) Take half the price or return the slave and take full price
C) Take full price only
D) Nothing, because the defect occurred after sale

Key Takeaway
If a slave is lost due to a cause that existed in the seller's possession, the buyer may reclaim the price, analogous to a usurper's liability.

Compensation for a Slave Who Steals in Both Parties' Possession
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation for a slave's theft in both possession periods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the buyer's compensation when a slave's hand is cut off for thefts in both the seller's and buyer's possession, and apply the proportional reasoning to determine the buyer's entitlement.

Core Idea
When a slave's hand is cut for two thefts, the buyer's compensation is halved because only the theft in the seller's possession is recoverable.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the allocation of a physical loss between seller and buyer. A slave commits theft in both parties' possession, leading to hand amputation. The key question: how much can the buyer claim from the seller? The answer turns on whether the buyer's own possession period introduced a new defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two thefts and the resulting loss.
The slave stole once while in the seller's possession and once while in the buyer's possession. As a result, the slave's hand is cut off. The hand is considered half the value of a person (slave).
The loss is indivisible physically but must be allocated legally.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning.
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the buyer can claim full compensation for the defect from the seller, as if only the theft in the seller's possession mattered. They treat the buyer's theft as irrelevant to the seller's liability.
This is the default rule for pre-existing defects.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning.
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the buyer cannot return the slave without the seller's consent because a new defect (the theft in the buyer's possession) arose. Instead, the buyer recovers only a quarter of the price (half of the hand's value, since only half the loss is attributable to the seller's theft). If the seller accepts the return, the buyer recovers three-quarters (because the quarter attributed to the buyer's theft is deducted).
Abu Hanifa uses proportional allocation: hand is halved, and the loss is halved between the two crimes.
4
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
The dispute results in different compensation amounts: under the Sahibayn, the buyer gets full defect compensation; under Abu Hanifa, the buyer gets only a quarter (or three-quarters if seller accepts return).
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi.
5
Evaluate the reasoning structure.
Abu Hanifa's reasoning uses qiyas: the hand is half the person, so the loss of the hand is apportioned equally to the two thefts. The buyer can only claim for the theft that occurred before purchase, so only half of the hand's value (quarter of price) is recoverable.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on proportion.

And if the slave stole in the seller's possession and then in the buyer's possession, and the hand was cut off due to both, according to them (Sahibayn) the buyer claims compensation for the defect as we mentioned. According to him (Abu Hanifa), he cannot return it without the seller's consent due to a new defect, and he takes one-fourth of the price; if the seller accepts, then three-fourths, because the hand of a person is half of him, and it was lost due to two crimes, and in one of them there is recourse, so it is halved.
Translation: If the slave stole in seller's possession and then in buyer's, and hand cut due to both, Sahibayn: buyer claims defect compensation. Abu Hanifa: cannot return without consent due to new defect; takes quarter of price; if seller accepts, takes three-quarters. Because a person's hand is half, and it was lost due to two crimes, and in one of them there is recourse, so it is halved. Explanation: The issue is that a slave stole once with seller, once with buyer, and hand cut. Sahibayn: buyer compensates for defect that arose with seller. Abu Hanifa: buyer cannot return hand-cut slave without consent because new defect (theft with buyer) occurred. But buyer can take quarter of price, since hand cut due to both thefts. If seller accepts return, buyer takes three-quarters because only one-quarter is attributed to the theft with buyer.

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a slave commits theft while in the seller's possession and again while in the buyer's possession, and as a result, his hand is cut off. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. The Sahibayn allow the buyer to claim compensation for the defect (the theft-related loss) from the seller, as in a typical case of a pre-existing defect. Imam Abu Hanifa, however, rules that because a new defect (the theft in the buyer's possession) arose, the buyer cannot return the slave without the seller's consent. Instead, the buyer can only recover a quarter of the price (half of the hand's value, since the hand is half of a person's value, and only half of that loss is attributable to the theft in the seller's possession). If the seller agrees to take back the slave, the buyer recovers three-quarters of the price, as the quarter attributable to the buyer's own theft is deducted. The reasoning is based on allocating the loss of the hand between the two thefts.

Key Terms
عیب (ʿayb): A defect in the sold item that existed at the time of sale or arose before delivery, entitling the buyer to return or compensation.
نقصان (nuqṣān): The compensation amount equal to the reduction in value due to the defect.

Recourse in Chain of Sales: The Hand-Cutting Dispute
States the ruling when a slave is sold through multiple buyers and hand cut off at last buyer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding recourse in a chain of sales when a defect (hand-cutting) occurs at the last buyer, and apply the correct Hanafi ruling to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
In a chain of sales, Abu Hanifa allows each buyer to claim from his seller; Sahibayn restrict to the last buyer.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the liability of sellers when a sold item (a slave) suffers a defect (hand-cutting) after passing through multiple hands. Imam Abu Hanifa treats the defect like a claim of ownership (istihqaq), allowing each buyer to recover the price from his immediate seller. The Sahibayn treat it as an ordinary defect, limiting recourse to the last buyer alone. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for determining who bears the loss in multi-party transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling: each buyer can claim his price from his seller, regardless of knowledge.
This is analogous to istihqaq (claim of ownership).
2
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn)
The Sahibayn hold that only the last buyer can claim from his seller, and that seller cannot pass the liability further back.
They treat the hand-cutting as a defect arising after the sale.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: analogy to istihqaq, where knowledge does not prevent recourse. Sahibayn's proof: classification as a defect, which only allows the last buyer to return.
The text also notes the effect of the buyer's knowledge.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is the extent of liability: under Abu Hanifa, all sellers are potentially liable; under Sahibayn, only the last seller bears the loss.
This affects who can recover the price in a chain.
5
Note the tarjih and effect of knowledge
The author's phrasing suggests support for the Sahibayn. However, the correct view is that knowledge of a claim (istihqaq) does not bar recourse per Abu Hanifa.
Knowledge of the defect would be consent according to Sahibayn, but not according to Abu Hanifa.

If the hands multiplied (i.e., the slave passed through several buyers) and then the hand was cut off in the hand of the last, according to him (Abu Hanifa) each buyer takes back from his seller, as in the case of istihqaq (claim). According to them (Sahibayn), the last buyer takes back from his seller, and his seller does not take back from his seller, because it is in the category of a defect. And the author's saying in the book: 'And the buyer did not know' benefits according to their school because knowledge of the unseen is consent, but it does not benefit according to his school in the correct view, because knowledge of the claim does not prevent recourse.
Translation: If this slave who stole was sold through several buyers, and then his hand was cut off in the possession of the last buyer, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa each buyer takes back his price from his seller, as in the case of istihqaq. According to the Sahibayn, the last buyer takes back from his seller, and his seller cannot take back from his seller, because it is in the category of a defect. And the author's saying in the book.

Commentary
This passage deals with a chain of sales: a slave who committed theft is sold through multiple buyers, and finally the hand is cut off at the last buyer. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. Imam Abu Hanifa analogizes this to the case of istihqaq (a third party claiming ownership), where each buyer can claim back the price from his immediate seller, regardless of whether the subsequent buyers knew of the claim. Thus, every seller in the chain is liable to his buyer. The Sahibayn, however, treat the cutting of the hand as a defect that arose after the sale to each subsequent buyer. Since it is a defect, only the last buyer can return to his seller, and that seller cannot pass the liability further back. The text also notes a disagreement about the effect of the buyer's knowledge: according to the Sahibayn, knowledge at the time of purchase would imply consent, preventing recourse; but according to Imam Abu Hanifa (in the correct view), knowledge of a claim (istihqaq) does not prevent the right to recourse.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): A third party's valid claim of ownership over the sold item, making the sale void ab initio.
عیب (ʿayb): A defect in the item; here, the Sahibayn treat the hand-cutting as a defect arising after sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) No one can claim because it is a new defect
D) The first seller is responsible to all buyers

Key Takeaway
In a chain of sales, the Hanafi school has two positions on recourse when a defect appears at the last buyer: Abu Hanifa allows all buyers to claim, while the Sahibayn restrict it to the last buyer.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on a Slave Who Stole Twice: Recourse for the Buyer
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slave's hand cut off due to two thefts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the buyer's recourse when a slave's hand is cut off due to thefts with both seller and buyer, and calculate the compensation under each view.

Core Idea
If a slave's hand is cut off due to thefts with both seller and buyer, Abu Hanifa gives a fixed fraction; the Sahibayn give actual value loss.

This passage presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. A slave steals once with the seller and once with the buyer, then his hand is cut off. The question is: what can the buyer recover from the seller? The two positions—Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's—differ fundamentally in whether the amputation is treated as a defect or as a legal disqualification (istihqaq), leading to different calculations. This lesson walks through the reasoning and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Identify the key facts: a slave stole once while with the seller and once after coming to the buyer; both thefts lead to hand amputation. The buyer now wants recourse from the seller.
The slave is the object of sale; the buyer suffers a loss due to the amputation.
2
Articulate the Sahibayn's view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) treat the hand amputation as a defect ('ayb). The buyer cannot return the slave because a new defect (second theft) arose with him. He can only recover the loss in value attributable to the first theft (the difference between a thief and a non-thief).
This is the standard defect rule: no return if a new defect occurs, but compensation for the pre-existing defect.
3
Articulate Abu Hanifa's view
Imam Abu Hanifa treats the amputation as akin to istihqaq (legal disqualification). The buyer cannot return the slave due to new defect, but he recovers a fixed fraction of the price: one-fourth if he keeps the slave, three-fourths if the seller takes back the slave.
The fraction is based on the fact that a hand is half the value of a slave, and half of that half (one-fourth) is attributed to the seller's theft.
4
Identify the core difference
The dispute hinges on the legal nature of hand amputation: is it a defect or an istihqaq? This determines whether compensation is a fixed fraction or actual value loss.
Abu Hanifa sees it as a legal consequence shared between parties; the Sahibayn see it as a physical defect.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, under Abu Hanifa's view, the buyer recovers a fixed 1/4 or 3/4 regardless of the actual price difference between a thief and a non-thief. Under the Sahibayn, compensation equals the actual depreciation from the first theft. This can lead to different amounts.
The mufta bihi is not stated in the text; later tarjih often prefers the Sahibayn's view.

مشتری کو نقصان ) واپس لینے کا حق ہے تو (اس آدھے کے دوٹکڑے ہو جائیں گے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ غلام صبیح نے ایک بار بائع کے پاس رہتے ہوئے چوری کی اور ایک بار مشتری کے پاس آکر چوری کی پھر دونوں چوریوں کی وجہ سے اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا گیا تو صاحبین کے نزدیک مشتری بائع سے اس عیب کا نقصان واپس لے گا جو بائع کے پاس چوری کرنے کی وجہ سے پیدا ہوا تھا یعنی چور اور غیر چور کی قیمتوں میں جو تفاوت ظاہر ہو گا مشتری اس کو بائع سے واپس لے گا جیسا کہ سابق میں بالتفصیل گذر چکا اور حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک مشتری ہاتھ کٹا ہوا اعلام بغیر بائع کی رضامندی کے واپس نہیں کر سکتا کیونکہ مشتری کے پاس اس غلام میں ایک نیا عیب پیدا ہو گیا ہے یعنی غلام کا مشتری کے پاس چوری کرنا اور دونوں چوریوں کی وجہ سے ہاتھ کتنا اور یہ بات پہلے گزر چکی کہ اگر مشتری کے پاس بیچ کے اندر کوئی نیا عیب پیدا ہو گیا ہو تو مشتری کو مبیع واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہیں رہتا اسلئے اس صورت میں بھی مشتری کو مقطوع الید غلام واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہ ہو گا۔ البتہ مشتری ایک چوتھائی شمن بائع سے واپس لے گا کیونکہ غلام کا ہاتھ دو چوریوں کی وجہ سے کاٹا گیا ہے۔ ایک وہ چوری جو بائع کے پاس کی ہے اور ایک وہ جومشتری کے پاس کی ہے اور یہ بات طے شدہ ہے کہ مشتری اس عیب کا نقصان بائع سے لے سکتا ہے جو عیب بائع کے پاس پیدا ہوا ہو نہ کہ اس عیب کا جو خود مشتری کے قبضہ میں پیدا ہوا ہے۔ پس جب غلام کا ہاتھ دونوں چوریوں کی وجہ سے کاٹا گیا ہے تو مشتری کو اسی قدرشن واپس لینے کا اختیار ہو گا جو ایک ہاتھ کے نصف کے مقابل آتا ہو اور ایک ہاتھ کے مقابل چونکہ نصف ثمن آتا ہے۔ اسلئے آدھے ہاتھ کے مقابل چوتھائی شمن آئے گا۔ پس مشتری کو اس چوری کی وجہ سے جو غلام نے بائع کے پاس کی ہے ایک چوتھائی شمن واپس لینے کا حق حاصل ہوگا۔ اور اگر بائع نے ہاتھ کٹا ہوا غلام واپس لینا چاہا تو اس صورت میں مشتری کو بائع سے تین چوتھائی شمن واپس لینے کا اختیار ہوگا کیونکہ آدمی کا ہاتھ آدمی کا نصف شمار ہوتا ہے۔ حالانکہ غلام کا ہاتھ دو چوریوں کی وجہ سے کاٹا گیا ہے اور مشتری کو ان دونوں میں سے ایک میں نقصان عیب لینے کا حق ہے تو اس آدھے کے دو نصف ہو جائیں گے اور آدھے کا ایک نصف چوتھائی ہوتا ہے۔ پس اگر نقصان عیب لینا قبول کر لیا تو مشتری بائع سے تین چوتھائی شمن واپس لے لے۔ کیونکہ ایک چوتھائی شمن اس چوری کی وجہ سے ساقط ہو گیا ہے جو چوری غلام نے خود مشتری کے پاس کی ہے۔
The buyer's right to recover loss: He has the right to take back (the slave), so that half will become two pieces. Explanation of the case: A slave who was a thief stole once while with the seller and once after coming to the buyer, then due to both thefts his hand was cut off. According to the Sahibayn, the buyer will recover from the seller the loss caused by the theft that occurred with the seller, i.e., the difference between the price of a thief and a non-thief. As for Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer cannot return the slave without the seller's consent, because a new defect arose with the buyer, i.e., the slave's theft with the buyer and the hand being cut off due to both thefts. It has passed earlier that if a new defect arises with the buyer, the buyer loses the right to return the goods. Therefore, in this case too, the buyer does not have the right to return the slave with the hand cut off. However, the buyer will recover one-fourth of the price from the seller, because the slave's hand was cut off due to two thefts: one committed with the seller and one with the buyer. It is established that the buyer can recover from the seller the loss for a defect that arose with the seller, not for a defect that arose in his own possession. So when the hand was cut off due to both thefts, the buyer has the right to recover only the portion corresponding to half of one hand, and since the value of a hand is half the price, half of that is one-fourth. Thus, the buyer is entitled to recover one-fourth of the price for the theft committed with the seller. If the seller wishes to take back the slave, then the buyer would recover three-fourths of the price, because a hand is considered half of a person. Since the hand was cut off due to two thefts and the buyer is entitled to recover for one theft, this half becomes two parts, and half of a half is one-fourth. So if he accepts to take the loss for the defect, the buyer will take three-fourths from the seller, because one-fourth is lost due to the theft committed with the buyer.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a slave who stole while in the seller's possession and again after coming into the buyer's possession, and then his hand is cut off as punishment. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) treat the hand amputation as a defect ('ayb) that existed at the time of sale (the first theft) and a new defect (the second theft). Therefore, they allow the buyer to recover from the seller only the loss attributable to the first theft, i.e., the difference in value between a thief and a non-thief. The buyer cannot return the slave because the new defect (second theft) prevents return. However, Imam Abu Hanifa treats the hand amputation as equivalent to a legal disqualification (istihqaq) rather than a defect. According to him, since the slave's hand is cut off due to causes from both parties, the buyer can recover a proportionate amount: one-fourth of the price if he keeps the slave, or three-fourths if the seller takes back the slave. This is based on the principle that a hand constitutes half of a person's value. The dispute hinges on whether the hand amputation is classified as a defect or as an istihqaq (legal claim).

Key Terms
خیار عیب (khiyar al-‘ayb): The option to return a defective item due to a pre-existing defect.
عیب حادث (‘ayb hadith): A new defect that arises after the buyer takes possession, which may prevent return.
استحقاق (istihqaq): A legal disqualification or claim that voids the sale, like finding the item belongs to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a slave's hand is cut off due to two thefts (one with seller, one with buyer) and the buyer keeps the slave, what fraction of the price does the buyer recover from the seller?
A) Half the price
B) One-fourth the price
C) Three-fourths the price
D) Full price

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute on a slave's hand cut off due to thefts with both parties, Abu Hanifa fixes compensation at 1/4 or 3/4 of the price, while the Sahibayn award actual value loss from the first theft.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Is Hand-Cutting Istihqaq or 'Ayb?
Intra-Hanafi dispute over recovery of price when slave's hand cut off

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Imam Abu Hanifa's istihqaq-based view and the Sahibayn's defect-based view regarding a slave's hand-cutting, and apply the ruling to determine which buyers in a chain can recover the price or defect compensation.

Core Idea
Hand-cutting is either istihqaq (full chain recourse) or a defect (only last buyer recovers)—classification dictates the entire claim structure.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute: when a slave's hand is cut off for theft committed before sale, is it a legal entitlement (istihqaq) or a physical defect ('ayb)? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ on this, leading to very different outcomes for a chain of buyers. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to classify legal events and trace the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A slave commits theft while with his first owner, then is sold sequentially to Hamid, Shakir, and Zahid. While with the last buyer (Zahid), his hand is cut off as punishment.
The hand-cutting occurs after the sales, but the theft was before the first sale.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's view
Hand-cutting is istihqaq (legal entitlement). Each buyer can return the slave and recover the full price from his seller, or keep the slave and recover half the price.
This is because the hand-cutting effectively removes the slave from the buyer's ownership, similar to a third-party claim.
3
State the Sahibayn's view
Hand-cutting is a defect ('ayb). Only the last buyer (Zahid) can claim defect compensation from his seller because he is not a habis (holder). Intermediate sellers (Shakir and Hamid) are habis and cannot claim.
A habis is someone who has resold the item; they lose the right to claim defect compensation from their own seller.
4
Analyse the key distinction
The difference hinges on whether the hand-cutting is likened to a third-party claim (istihqaq) or a physical flaw (defect). This determines the chain of recourse.
Imam Abu Hanifa focuses on the legal consequence; the Sahibayn focus on the physical change.
5
Apply the condition 'wa lam ya'lam'
The matn (al-Jami' al-Saghir) states 'the buyer did not know.' This condition is relevant only for the Sahibayn: if the buyer knew of the theft, he would be deemed to have accepted the defect, waiving khiyar al-ayb. For Imam Abu Hanifa, knowledge does not waive the right because istihqaq is not a defect.
Thus, the matn's wording supports the Sahibayn's view over Imam Abu Hanifa's.

حابس للمبیع ہو تو اس کو نقصان عیب واپس لینے کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا خواہ اس کو مبیع کے اندر پیدا شدہ عیب کا علم ہو یا علم نہ ہو اور اگر مشتری حابس للمبیع نہ ہو اور مبیع واپس کرنا بھی متعذر نہ ہو تو وہ عیب
