صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ متن یعنی جامع صغیر میں امام محمد کا قول و لم يعلم المشتری ( یعنی غلام کا چور ہونا مشتری کو نہ بیع کے وقت معلوم ہے اور نہ قبضہ کے وقت ) صاحبین کے قول پر تو مفید ہے لیکن امام صاحب کے صحیح قول پر مفید نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ صاحبین کے نزدیک چوری کی وجہ سے ہاتھ کٹنے کا وجوب بمنزلہ عیب کے ہے اور مشتری کا خریدتے وقت یا قبضہ کے وقت مبیع کے عیب سے واقف ہونا مشتری کی عیب پر رضامندی شمار ہوتی ہے اور مبیع کے عیب پر مشتری کی رضامندی اس کے خیار عیب کو ساقط کر دیتی ہے اسلئے خیار عیب باقی رہنے کے واسطے مشتری کی رضامندی اس کے خیار عیب کو ساقط کر دیتی ہے اسلئے خیار عیب باقی رہنے کے واسطے مشتری کا عیب سے لاعلم رہنا ضروری ہے اور امام صاحب کے نزدیک چونکہ سرقہ کی وجہ سے ہاتھ کاٹا جانا بمنزلہ استحقاق کے ہے اور مشتری کو اگر مبیع کے مستحق ہونے کا علم ہو جائے تو یہ علم رجوع بالنقصان کو منع نہیں کرتا۔ اسلئے ولم يعلم بہ کی قید امام صاحب کے قول پر مفید نہ ہوگی۔ یعنی امام صاحب کے نزدیک مشتری کی چوری کا علم ہو یا علم نہ ہو۔ دونوں صورتوں میں اس کو رجوع بالنقصان کا حق حاصل ہوگا۔
Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Hidayah, Urdu commentary on Hidayah, volume eight. And the statement 'wa lam ya'lam al-mushtari' (the buyer did not know) is beneficial according to the madhhab of the Sahibayn because being aware of the defect constitutes consent to the defect. And according to the correct (musih) saying, it will not be beneficial according to Imam Sahib's saying because being aware of the entitlement (istihqaq) does not prevent taking back the price. Explanation: The scenario of the issue is that a slave committed theft while in the possession of his master. Then Hamid bought him, and Shakir bought from Hamid, and Zahid bought from Shakir. Then, in the possession of the last buyer, Zahid, his hand was cut off due to the same theft he had committed while with the first seller. So the madhhab of Hadrat Imam Abu Hanifa is that every buyer can return the hand-cut slave and take his full price from his seller, or keep the hand-cut slave and take half the price from his seller. That is, Zahid takes from Shakir, Shakir takes from Hamid, and Hamid takes from the first seller in whose possession the slave committed theft. Because according to Hadrat Imam Sahib, the cutting of the slave's hand is in the rank of entitlement (istihqaq), and the ruling of entitlement is that if the slave becomes entitled (mustahaqq) in the possession of the last buyer, then every buyer takes back the price from his seller. Similarly, in the case of hand-cutting, every buyer will take back the price from his seller. And the madhhab of the Sahibayn is that the last buyer, Zahid, can take back the defect compensation (nuqsan al-ayb) from his seller, Shakir, but Zahid's seller, Shakir, cannot take back defect compensation from his seller, Hamid. Because hand-cutting, according to the Sahibayn, is in the rank of a defect, and the earlier rule has passed that if the buyer is a holder (habis) of the sold item, he does not have the option to take back defect compensation, whether he knew of the defect that arose in the sold item or not. And if the buyer is not a holder of the sold item and returning the sold item is not impossible, then he can return the sold item due to the defect. And if returning the sold item becomes impossible due to a new defect, then the buyer is permitted to take back defect compensation. So, since the last buyer, Zahid, did not sell this slave, he is not a holder. And since the slave's hand was cut while in the possession of the last buyer, and according to the Sahibayn, hand-cutting is a new defect ('ayb hadith) that made returning the sold item impossible, therefore the last buyer can take back defect compensation from his seller. And since the last buyer's seller, Shakir, sold this slave, therefore both Shakir and his seller, Hamid, became holders. And since a holder does not have the option to take back defect compensation from his seller, therefore the last buyer's seller cannot take back defect compensation from his seller, and he cannot from his seller, Hamid. The author of Hidayah says: The text, meaning al-Jami' al-Saghir, has the statement of Imam Muhammad: 'wa lam ya'lam al-mushtari' (and the buyer did not know) — i.e., the slave being a thief is not known to the buyer at the time of sale nor at the time of possession. This statement is beneficial according to the saying of the Sahibayn, but it is not beneficial according to the correct saying of Imam Sahib. Because, according to the Sahibayn, the obligation of hand-cutting due to theft is like a defect. And the buyer's being aware of the defect at the time of purchase or possession counts as the buyer's consent to the defect. And the buyer's consent to the defect of the sold item nullifies his option of defect (khiyar al-ayb). Therefore, for the option of defect to remain, it is necessary that the buyer be unaware of the defect. And according to Imam Sahib, since hand-cutting due to theft is like entitlement, and if the buyer comes to know that the sold item is entitled, this knowledge does not prevent recourse for compensation (ruju' bi al-nuqsan). Therefore, the restriction 'wa lam ya'lam bihi' will not be beneficial according to Imam Sahib's saying. That is, according to Imam Sahib, whether the buyer knows of the theft or not, in both cases he has the right to recourse for compensation.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage explains an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding multiple sales of a slave whose hand is cut off due to theft committed before the first sale. The author begins by noting that the phrase 'wa lam ya'lam al-mushtari' in the matn (al-Jami' al-Saghir) supports the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) but not that of Imam Abu Hanifa. The passage then details the scenario: a slave steals from his original owner, then is sold sequentially to Hamid, Shakir, and Zahid. The last buyer Zahid's hand is cut off as punishment. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the cutting off of the hand is equivalent to istihqaq (the slave becoming legally entitled to someone else), so each buyer can recover the full price from his seller by returning the slave, or keep the slave and recover half the price. The Sahibayn treat the hand-cutting as a defect ('ayb). Since the last buyer Zahid still possesses the slave and did not sell him, he is not a habis (holder) and can claim defect compensation from his seller (Shakir). However, Shakir and Hamid are holders because they resold the slave, so they cannot claim from their sellers. The Hidayah commentator clarifies that the matn's condition 'the buyer did not know' is relevant only for the Sahibayn: if the buyer knew of the defect, his khiyar al-ayb is waived. For Imam Abu Hanifa, knowledge of istihqaq does not waive the right to recourse, so the condition is superfluous.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqaq): The state of being legally entitled to something; here, the slave becomes mustahaqq (entitled to be taken) due to the hand-cutting as a punishment, similar to a case where the slave is found to be owned by another.
حابس للمبیع (habis lil-mabi'): A buyer who is a 'holder' of the sold item, meaning he has resold it or otherwise retained it, thereby losing the right to claim defect compensation.
رجوع بالنقصان (ruju' bi al-nuqsan): Recourse for compensation of the defect; the buyer's right to recover a portion of the price due to a defect in the sold item that cannot be returned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn in this passage, why can the intermediate seller Shakir not claim defect compensation from his seller Hamid?
A) Because he is a habis lil-mabi' (holder) since he resold the slave.
B) Because he knew of the defect at the time of sale.
C) Because the hand-cutting is considered istihqaq, not a defect.
D) Because the hand-cutting occurred after he sold the slave.

Key Takeaway
The classification of a legal event as istihqaq or 'ayb fundamentally alters the rights of successive buyers in a chain of sales.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Validity of Bara'a: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute on bara'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates immunity from unknown defects in a sale contract, evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi positions on coverage.

Core Idea
Bara'a is isqaat, not tamleek — ignorance in waivers does not invalidate.

This lesson examines a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over a sale contract in which the seller stipulates immunity from all defects. The Hanafi school holds that the buyer cannot return the item for any defect, based on the view that immunity is a waiver of rights (isqaat), not a transfer of ownership (tamleek). The Shafi'i school argues that such immunity is invalid because it involves an unknown right. We will work through the proofs, the Hanafi rebuttal, and an intra-Hanafi disagreement about what defects are covered.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the sale with a bara'a clause is valid and the buyer cannot return the item for any defect, even if unknown.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the immunity is invalid; either the sale is fasid or the condition is void, so the buyer retains the right of return.
The Shafi'i argument rests on the idea that immunity is tamleek (ownership transfer), which requires knowledge of the thing owned.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i's dalil: If a creditor immunizes a debtor from a debt, and the debtor rejects it, the immunity is revoked — this shows immunity is tamleek. Tamleek of an unknown thing is invalid.
The analogy is from debt immunity to defect immunity.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that bara'a is isqaat, not tamleek. Ignorance in isqaat does not lead to dispute because no delivery is required. The revocation upon rejection is not exclusive to tamleek; it can occur in waivers too.
The Hanafi dalil: jahalah in isqaat does not cause munaza'ah.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Hanafi: sale valid, buyer cannot return. Shafi'i: sale either fasid or the condition is void, buyer can return for defects.
Also note the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf includes defects arising before delivery; Muhammad and Zufar exclude them.

Meaning: A slave sold on the condition that I am immune from all defects, then it cannot be returned. He said: And whoever sells a slave and stipulates immunity from every defect, then he does not have the right to return it due to a defect, even if he did not enumerate the defects by number. And al-Shafi'i said: The immunity is not valid, based on his madhhab that immunity from unknown rights is not valid. He says that in immunity there is ownership (tamleek) such that it is revoked by rejection, and ownership of an unknown thing is not valid. And our evidence is that ignorance in waiver (isqaat) does not lead to dispute, even if it implies ownership, because there is no need for delivery, so it is not harmful. And according to the saying of Abu Yusuf, this immunity includes the existing defect and the one arising before delivery. And Muhammad said the one arising before delivery is not included, and this is also the saying of Zufar, because immunity covers what is established. And Abu Yusuf's evidence is that the purpose is to make the contract binding by waiving his right to the quality of soundness, and this is achieved by immunity from both existing and arising defects. Translation: He said: And whoever sold a slave and stipulated immunity from every defect, then the buyer does not have the right to return the sold item due to any defect, even if he did not enumerate the defects by name. And Imam al-Shafi'i said that this immunity is not valid, based on his madhhab that immunity from unknown rights is not valid. He says that in immunity there is the meaning of ownership, such that it is revoked by rejection, and ownership of an unknown thing is not valid. And our evidence is that ignorance in waiver does not lead to dispute, even if it implies ownership, because there is no need for delivery, so such ignorance is not corrupting. And according to the saying of Abu Yusuf, this immunity includes every defect that actually exists at the time of contract and the defect that arises before delivery. And Imam Muhammad said that the one arising before delivery is not included, and Imam Zufar's saying is also this, because immunity covers what is established. And Abu Yusuf's evidence is that the purpose of immunity is to make the sale binding by waiving the buyer's right to the quality of soundness, and this purpose is achieved by immunity from both existing and arising defects. Explanation: The scenario of the issue is that if someone sold a slave and stipulated immunity from every defect, i.e., the seller said, 'I am immune from every defect of the slave,' and the buyer accepted this condition, then now the buyer does not have the right to return the slave due to any defect, whether the defects were enumerated by name or not, and whether the seller or buyer knew of those defects or not. Imam al-Shafi'i said that this immunity is not valid, i.e., the sale with the condition of immunity from every defect is fasid (corrupt). And one narration from Imam al-Shafi'i is that in this case, the sale is valid but the condition is void. The basis of Imam al-Shafi'i's saying is his madhhab that immunity from unknown rights is not valid, because in immunity there is the meaning of ownership. This is why if a creditor immunizes his debtor from the debt, but the debtor rejects it, then it is revoked by his rejection, i.e., after the debtor's rejection, the immunity is not valid. And being revoked by rejection is a sign of being from the category of ownership (tamleekat). So it is known that immunity is from the category of tamleekat, and since ownership of an unknown thing is not valid, therefore immunity from unknown defects is not valid. Our evidence is that immunity is from the category of waiver (isqaat), not from the category of ownership.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a sale contract where the seller stipulates immunity (bara'a) from all defects, meaning the buyer cannot return the item due to any defect. The author begins by citing the matn: if a slave is sold with such a condition, the buyer cannot return it even if defects are not enumerated. Then a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i is presented. Al-Shafi'i's opinion is that such immunity is invalid because it involves immunity from unknown rights, which he considers a form of ownership (tamleek) that requires knowledge. He analogizes it to a creditor immunizing a debtor from an unknown debt, which would be invalid because the debtor's rejection would revoke it, showing it is ownership. The Hanafi response is that immunity is a waiver (isqaat), not ownership. Ignorance in waiver does not lead to dispute because there is no need for delivery. Therefore, the immunity is valid. After establishing the validity, the passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute about what defects are covered by the immunity. Abu Yusuf holds that it covers both existing defects and defects arising before delivery (qabd). Muhammad and Zufar hold that it only covers existing defects because immunity applies only to what is established at the time of the contract. Abu Yusuf argues that the purpose is to make the contract binding by waiving the buyer's right to a sound item, which requires immunity from both existing and future defects up to delivery. The Urdu commentary further explains the Shafi'i position and the Hanafi rebuttal, emphasizing that bara'a is isqaat, not tamleek, and thus valid even for unknown defects.

Key Terms
براءة (bara'a): Immunity or disclaimer from defects; the seller's declaration that he is not liable for any defects in the sold item.
اسقاط (isqaat): Waiver or relinquishment of a right; here, the seller waives his right to claim immunity, or the buyer waives his right to return the item.
تملیک (tamleek): Transfer of ownership; the Shafi'i claim that bara'a involves transferring a right to the buyer, requiring knowledge of the right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to al-Shafi'i, why does ignorance in immunity not invalidate the bara'a clause?
A) Because the seller enumerates all defects.
B) Because immunity is a waiver (isqaat) and ignorance in waivers does not lead to dispute.
C) Because the buyer's acceptance cures the ignorance.
D) Because the sale would be invalid otherwise.

Key Takeaway
Bara'a is isqaat, not tamleek — ignorance in waivers does not invalidate; the buyer cannot return the item for any defect covered by the clause.

intermediate

The Validity and Scope of a Condition of Immunity from Defects (Barā'a)
Resolves Barā'a ruling, Shafi'i objection, Hanafi response, and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a sale with immunity from all defects, evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument, and distinguish the intra-Hanafi positions on the scope of such immunity.

Core Idea
Immunity from defects is isqāt (dropping of a right), not tamlīk, so ignorance does not invalidate it.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a sale where the seller stipulates immunity from all defects. The Hanafi madhhab holds such a condition valid, while Imam al-Shafi'i considers it invalid because it involves unknown rights. We will explore the legal reasoning behind each position and also examine an internal Hanafi disagreement about whether the immunity covers defects arising after the contract but before delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Opposition
State the Hanafi position: the sale is valid and the buyer cannot return the item. Note the Shafi'i objection that immunity from unknown defects is invalid because it amounts to tamlīk of an unknown thing.
2
Analyze the Hanafi Proof
Understand the key distinction between isqāt (dropping of a right) and tamlīk (conferral of ownership). Recognize that ignorance (jahāla) in isqāt does not lead to dispute because no delivery is required, so it is not corruptive.
This is a rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) based on the nature of rights.
3
Follow the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Determine that according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, immunity covers both existing defects and those arising before delivery. Muhammad and Zufar restrict it to defects existing at the time of contract. Identify the mufta bihi (operative) position.
4
Evaluate the Practical Consequence
Note the thamarah al-khilaf: in the Hanafi view, the buyer loses the right to return for any defect; in the Shafi'i view, either the sale is invalid or the condition is void, preserving the buyer's option. The intra-Hanafi difference affects post-contract pre-delivery defects.

فرمایا ہے کہ یہ براءت صحیح نہیں ہے یعنی ہر عيب سے بری ہونے کی شرط کے ساتھ بیع فاسد ہے اور امام شافعی کی ایک روایت یہ ہے کہ اس صورت میں بیع تو صحیح ہے لیکن شرط باطل ہے۔ امام شافعی کے قول کی بنیاد ان کے اس کے رد کرنے لئے ہو جائے گا۔ یعنی قرضدار کے رد کر دینے کے بعد ابراء (بری کرنا ) صحیح نہ ہو گا ۔ اور رد کرنے سے رو ہو جانا از قبیلۂ تملیک ہونے کی علامت ہے۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ بری کرنا تملیکات کے قبیلہ سے ہے اور مجہول چیز کی تملیک چونکہ صحیح نہیں ہے اس لئے مجھبول عیوب سے بری کرنا تیخ نہ ہوگا۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بری کرنا اسقاط کے قبیلہ سے ہے نہ کہ تملیک کے قبیلہ سے ہے کیونکہ لفظ ابراء' کے ساتھ تملیک عین درست نہیں ہے۔ پس جب لفظ ابراء کے ساتھ عین شی کا مالک کرنا درست نہیں ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ ابراء ( بری کرنا) تملیک نہیں ہے بلکہ اسقاط ہے اور جہالت اسقاطات میں مفضی الی المنازعہ نہیں ہوتی۔ کیونکہ جہالت براءت میں یعنی بائع کے ہر عيب سے براءت طلب کرنے کی صورت میں وہ عيب بھی داخل ہوگا جو عقد بیع کے وقت مبیع میں موجود ہے اور وہ عيب بھی داخل ہو گا جو عقد بیع کے بعد قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے پیدا ہوا ہے۔ یہی امام ابو حنیفہ کا مذہب اور امام ابو یوسف کی ایک روایت ہے اور امام محمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ جو عيب عقد بیع کے بعد اور قبضہ سے پہلے پیدا ہوا ہو۔ وہ اس برآو ت میں داخل نہ ہوگا۔ یعنی اس عيب کی وجہ سے مشتری کو خیار عيب حاصل ہوگا۔ یہی امام زفر ، امام شافعی اور امام مالک کا قول ہے۔ امام محمد کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ براءت ایسی چیز کو شامل ہوتی ہے جو ثابت ہو اور ثابت وہ عيب ہوتا ہے جو بیع کے وقت موجود ہو۔ اسلئے براءت فقط کہ مشتری کو جو سلیم مبیع کا حق تھاوہ ساقط کر کے بیج لازم کی جائے اور یه مقصود اسی وقت حاصل ہوگا جبکہ بائع موجود اور حادث دونوں طرح کے عیوب سے بری ہو۔ اسلئے اس براءت میں عيب موجود اور عيب حادث دونوں داخل ہوں گے۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
Book of Sales. He said: And whoever sells a slave and stipulates immunity from every defect, then he has no right to return it due to a defect, even if he did not enumerate the defects by name. Al-Shafi'i said: The immunity is not valid, based on his madhhab that immunity from unknown rights is not valid. He says that in immunity there is conferral of ownership (tamlīk) such that it is invalidated by rejection, and conferral of ownership of an unknown thing is invalid. Our proof is that ignorance (jahāla) in dropping of rights (isqāt) does not lead to dispute, even if it implies conferral of ownership, due to the absence of need for delivery; thus it is not corruptive. Included in this immunity is the existing defect and the defect arising before delivery according to Abu Yusuf's statement. Muhammad said: The arising defect is not included, and this is the statement of Zufar, because immunity applies only to what is established. Abu Yusuf's (proof) is that the purpose is to bind the contract by dropping his right to the description of soundness, and that is achieved by immunity from existing and arising defects. Translation: He said: And whoever sold a slave and stipulated immunity from every defect, then the buyer has no right to return the sold item due to any defect, even if he did not mention the defects individually. Imam Shafi'i said that this immunity is not valid, building on his madhhab that immunity from unknown rights is not permissible. He says that in immunity there is the meaning of conferring ownership, such that it is invalidated by rejection, and conferring ownership of an unknown thing is not valid. Our proof is that ignorance in dropping of rights does not lead to dispute. Even though it necessarily implies conferring ownership, because there is no need for delivery. Therefore such ignorance will not be corruptive. And included in this immunity is every defect that actually exists, according to the saying of Abu Yusuf, and also the defect that arises before delivery. Imam Muhammad said that what arises before delivery is not included, and Imam Zufar's saying is also this, because immunity covers what is established. Abu Yusuf's proof is that the purpose of immunity is to make the sale binding by dropping the buyer's right to the description of soundness, and this purpose will be achieved through immunity from both existing and arising defects. Commentary: The scenario is that if someone sold a slave and stipulated immunity from every defect, i.e., the seller said, 'I am immune from every defect of the slave,' and the buyer accepted this condition, then the buyer will not have the right to return the slave due to any defect, whether the defects were enumerated by name or not, whether the seller or buyer knew of those defects or not. Imam Shafi'i said that this immunity is not valid, i.e., the sale with the condition of immunity from every defect is invalid, and there is a narration from Imam Shafi'i that in this case the sale is valid but the condition is void. The basis of Imam Shafi'i's statement is his madhhab that immunity from unknown rights is not valid, because immunity contains the meaning of conferring ownership. This is why if a creditor absolves his debtor of a debt but the debtor rejects it, it becomes invalid upon his rejection. That is, after the debtor's rejection, the absolution (ibrā') is not valid. And that it is nullified by rejection is a sign of it being from the category of conferral of ownership (tamlīk). Thus it became known that absolution is from the category of conferrals, and since conferral of an unknown thing is not valid, therefore immunity from unknown defects will not be valid. Our proof is that absolution is from the category of dropping of rights (isqāt), not from the category of conferral, because the word ibrā' is not valid for conferral of the very thing. So when conferral of the very thing is not valid with the word ibrā', it became known that ibrā' (absolution) is not tamlīk but isqāt. And ignorance in matters of isqāt does not lead to dispute, because ignorance leads to dispute only when there is a need for delivery, and in isqāt there is no need for delivery. Therefore, ignorance in isqāt will not lead to dispute. And since ignorance in isqāt does not lead to dispute, it will not be corruptive of the contract. Hence we say that immunity from unknown defects is valid. Response to Imam Shafi'i's proof: In 'wa in kāna fī dimnihi al-tamlīk', the answer to Imam Shafi'i's proof 'hattā yartaddu bi al-radd' is given. The gist of the answer is that although ibrā' is isqāt, it implies tamlīk, so because tamlīk is implied, it is nullified by rejection. The author of Hidayah says: In this immunity, i.e., regarding the seller's immunity from every defect, the defect that was present at the time of the sale contract and the defect that arises after the contract but before delivery are included. This is the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa and a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad said that a defect arising after the contract and before delivery is not included in this immunity, meaning that the buyer will have the option of defect (khiyār al-'ayb) due to that defect. This is also the saying of Imam Zufar, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Malik. Imam Muhammad's proof is that immunity applies to what is established, and established is the defect present at the time of sale. Therefore, immunity will only include the defect present at the time of the contract, and will not include the defect arising after the contract and before delivery. Imam Abu Yusuf's proof is that the purpose of this immunity is to drop the buyer's right to a sound sold item and to make the sale binding, and this purpose will only be achieved when the seller is immune from both existing and arising defects. Hence, in this immunity, both existing and arising defects are included. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf concerning the validity and scope of a condition of immunity (barā'a) from all defects in a sale. The author begins by stating the Hanafi ruling: if a seller sells a slave and stipulates immunity from every defect, the buyer cannot return the item due to any defect, even if defects are not enumerated. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that such immunity is invalid because it involves absolution (ibrā') from unknown rights, which he considers a form of tamlīk (conferral of ownership) that is invalid when the object is unknown. The Hanafi response distinguishes ibrā' as isqāt (dropping of rights), not tamlīk. Isqāt does not require delivery, so ignorance (jahāla) in isqāt does not lead to dispute and is not corruptive. The author then explains the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: according to Abu Yusuf (and Abu Hanifa), the immunity includes both defects existing at the time of contract and those arising before delivery. Muhammad and Zufar argue that immunity only covers defects existing at the time of contract; subsequent defects are not included because immunity applies only to what is established (thābit). The passage concludes with the preferred Hanafi position that both types of defects are covered, making the sale binding and precluding return.

Key Terms
براءة (barā'a): Immunity from defects stipulated by the seller, releasing the seller from liability for defects.
إبراء (ibrā'): Absolution or release from a right or claim.
إسقاط (isqāt): Dropping or relinquishing a right.
تمليك (tamlīk): Conferral of ownership.
جهالة (jahāla): Ignorance or uncertainty about the object or right.
مفسد (mufsid): Corruptive factor that invalidates a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, which defects are covered by a condition of immunity from all defects according to the mufta bihi position?
A) Only defects existing at the time of contract.
B) Defects existing at contract and defects arising before delivery.
C) Only defects known to the seller.
D) All defects except those caused by the buyer.

Key Takeaway
A sale conditioned on immunity from all defects is valid in the Hanafi school because the immunity is isqāt (dropping of a right), not tamlīk, and ignorance in isqāt is not corruptive.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Four Types of Non-Valid Sales in Hanafi Fiqh
Introduces four categories of non-valid sales in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four categories of non-valid sales (bāṭil, mawqūf, fāsid, makrūh) and identify correct examples for each.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh splits non-valid sales into four distinct types: bāṭil, mawqūf, fāsid, makrūh – each with unique legal effects.

After understanding valid sales, it is crucial to recognize when a sale fails. This lesson introduces the four categories of non-valid sales according to the Hanafi school. You will learn the defining features and examples of each, providing a foundation for more detailed contract rulings.

Guided Path
1
List the four categories
Memorize the Arabic terms: bāṭil, mawqūf, fāsid, makrūh. These are the headings under which all invalid sales fall in Hanafi fiqh.
Each category has a distinct legal effect.
2
Define each category
For each term, understand what makes a sale belong to that category. Bāṭil: not legislated in essence or description. Mawqūf: valid in essence but contingent on ratification. Fāsid: valid in essence but defective in a non-essential condition. Makrūh: legally valid but religiously discouraged.
Focus on the essence (dhāt) vs description (wasf) distinction for bāṭil vs fāsid.
3
Distinguish bāṭil from fāsid
Bāṭil is void ab initio and cannot become valid. Fāsid can be rectified if the defect is removed (e.g., removing riba). This is a key Hanafi nuance.
Other schools may treat these differently.
4
Recognize examples for each
Bāṭil: sale of carrion (not property). Mawqūf: sale by a minor without guardian approval. Fāsid: sale with an unknown price (gharar) or riba. Makrūh: sale after the Friday adhan during prayer time.
Ensure you can classify new examples correctly.
5
Understand legal consequences
Bāṭil: no transfer of ownership. Mawqūf: ownership transfers upon ratification. Fāsid: if possession taken, rulings apply (e.g., option of confirmation). Makrūh: valid but sinful to engage in.
Consequences determine practical fiqh actions.

باب البيع الفاسد. ترجمه: يہ باب بيج فاسد کے بيان ميں ہے. جميل احمد عفي عنہ. تشريح: بيج صحيح کی دونوں قسموں یعنی لازم اور غير لازم کے بيان سے فارغ ہو کر اس باب ميں بيج غير صحيح کے احکام کا ذکر کيا گيا ہے۔ بيج غير صحيح کی چار قسميں ہيں۔ -۱ بيج باطل -۲ بيج موقوف -۳ بيج فاسد -۴ بيج مکروه. بيج باطل وہ ہے جو ذات اور وصف دونوں اعتبار سے مشروع نہ ہو جيسے معدوم کی بيج يا غير مال مردار وغيره کی بيج اور بيج فاسد وہ ہے
Chapter on the Invalid Sale. Translation: This chapter is about the invalid sale (bay' fāsid). Jameel Ahmad, may Allah forgive him. Commentary: Having completed the discussion of the two types of valid sales, i.e., binding (lāzim) and non-binding (ghayr lāzim), in this chapter the rulings of non-valid sales are mentioned. Non-valid sales are of four types: 1. Bāṭil sale, 2. Mawqūf sale, 3. Fāsid sale, 4. Makrūh sale. The bāṭil sale is that which is not legislated (mashrū') in terms of both essence (dhāt) and description (wasf), like the sale of a non-existent (ma'dūm) or the sale of a non-wealth (ghayr māl) like carrion, etc. And the fāsid sale is that...
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a definition and introduction to the chapter on invalid sales (bāb al-bay' al-fāsid). It begins by stating the purpose of the chapter: after discussing valid sales (lāzim and ghayr lāzim), it now covers rulings for non-valid sales. It enumerates four types: 1) Bāṭil (void ab initio): a sale that is not legislated in essence or description, e.g., selling a non-existent item or carrion (which is not considered property/māl). 2) Mawqūf (suspended): a sale that is valid in essence but contingent on ratification. 3) Fāsid (defective): a sale that is valid in essence but violates a condition not essential to the contract. 4) Makrūh (disliked): a sale that is legally valid but religiously disliked due to some prohibited aspect. The passage provides an example of bāṭil: sale of a non-existent thing or non-wealth like carrion. The distinction between bāṭil and fāsid is crucial in Hanafi fiqh: bāṭil cannot be rectified, while fāsid may become valid if the defect is removed.

Key Terms
بيج باطل (bay' bāṭil): A void sale, not legislated in essence (dhāt) or description (wasf).
بيج موقوف (bay' mawqūf): A suspended sale, valid in essence but dependent on ratification (e.g., by a guardian).
بيج فاسد (bay' fāsid): An invalid sale due to a defect in a non-essential condition.
بيج مکروه (bay' makrūh): A disliked sale, legally valid but discouraged due to some prohibition (e.g., selling after Friday adhan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this passage, which of the following is an example of a bāṭil (void) sale?
A) Sale of carrion (maytah).
B) Sale with a condition of immunity from defects.
C) Sale by a minor without guardian permission.
D) Sale of fruit before it ripens.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes four categories of non-valid sales: bāṭil (void), mawqūf (suspended), fāsid (defective but curable), and makrūh (valid but disliked).

Scope of Bara'at: Existing vs. Subsequent Defects
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of bara'at

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether bara'at covers defects arising after contract but before delivery, and explain the intra-Hanafi proofs.

Core Idea
Bara'at covers both existing and subsequent defects — the sale is binding with full discharge.

This passage addresses a key dispute within the Hanafi school: when a seller declares bara'at (discharge) from all defects in a sold item, does it include defects that appear after the contract but before delivery? The answer determines whether the buyer retains the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb). We will examine the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa, Imam Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad, along with their rational proofs, and determine the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal nature
First, understand that bara'at (ibra') is an isqat (dropping of a right), not a tamlik (transfer of ownership). This distinction is crucial because ignorance does not invalidate isqat as it does tamlik.
This answers the Shafi'i objection that ibra' requires precision.
2
Define the scope issue
The question is: when a seller declares bara'at from 'all defects', does it cover defects that arise after the contract but before delivery, or only those existing at the time of the contract?
3
Present the Hanafi positions
Imam Abu Hanifa and one narration of Abu Yusuf hold that both existing and subsequent defects are covered. Imam Muhammad, Imam Zufar, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Malik hold that only existing defects are covered.
Abu Yusuf has another narration aligning with Muhammad, but the majority view is the broader one.
4
Analyse the proofs
Imam Muhammad argues that bara'at only applies to what is 'established' at contract time. Abu Yusuf argues that the purpose of bara'at is to make the sale binding, which requires covering future defects as well.
The Hanafi school weighs the purpose (maqsad) over literal meaning.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the broader view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. A defect that appears after contract but before delivery is included in the discharge, and the buyer loses the option of defect.
This is the position recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.

So when making someone owner of a specific thing with the word 'ibra' is not valid, it becomes known that ibra (discharge) is not tamlik but isqat, and ignorance in isqatat does not lead to dispute. Because ignorance leads to dispute when there is a need to hand over something, and in isqatat there is no need to hand over. Therefore, ignorance in isqatat will also not lead to dispute. And when ignorance in isqatat does not lead to dispute, it will not be a corrupting factor in sale. Therefore we said that discharging from inherent defects is valid. The answer to Imam Shafi'i's proof: The phrase 'wa in kana fi dimnihi al-tamlik' is the answer to Imam Shafi'i's proof based on 'hatta yartadda bi al-radd'. The summary of the answer is that ibra, although it is isqat, has tamlik within it; so since the meaning of tamlik is found implicitly, ibra is rejected by rejection. Author of Hidayah says: In this bara'at, i.e., in the case of the seller seeking discharge from every defect, that defect which existed in the sold item at the time of the sale contract is included, and also the defect which arose after the contract but before possession. This is the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa and one narration from Imam Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad said: The defect that arose after the sale contract and before possession will not be included in this bara'at; that is, due to that defect the buyer will have the option of defect. This is the saying of Imam Zufar, Imam Shafi'i, and Imam Malik. Imam Muhammad's proof: Bara'at includes only that which is established, and the established defect is that which existed at the time of sale. Therefore, bara'at includes only the defect that existed at the time of the contract, and will not include the defect that arose after the contract and before possession. Imam Abu Yusuf's proof: The purpose of this bara'at is to nullify the buyer's right to a defect-free sold item and to make the sale binding, and this purpose is achieved only when the seller is discharged from both existing and subsequent defects. Therefore, in this bara'at, both existing and subsequent defects are included. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of bara'at (discharge) from defects in a sale. The context is a seller seeking to be discharged from all defects in the sold item. The core issue is whether this discharge includes only defects present at the time of the contract, or also defects that arise after the contract but before delivery. Imam Abu Hanifa and a narration from Abu Yusuf hold that both are included; the sale becomes binding without any option for the buyer. Imam Muhammad holds that only pre-existing defects are covered; defects arising later still give the buyer the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb). The text first establishes that ibra is an isqat (dropping of a right) rather than a tamlik (transfer of ownership), so ignorance in the subject matter does not invalidate it. This answers Imam Shafi'i's objection that ibra is a form of tamlik and thus must be precise. The Hidayah's author then states the ruling, attributing the broader view to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and the narrower to Muhammad, Zufar, Shafi'i, and Malik. Muhammad's rationale is that bara'at only applies to what is established, and only defects existing at contract time are established. Abu Yusuf's rationale is that the purpose of the bara'at is to make the sale binding, which requires covering future defects as well. The authoritative position (mufta bihi) is the broader view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as it is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ابراء (ibra'): Discharge or release from a right or claim; in this context, the seller's declaration of being free from all defects in the sold item.
اسقاط (isqat): The dropping or relinquishment of a right; here contrasted with tamlik as the nature of ibra'.
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of the buyer to return a defective item; this right is lost if bara'at covers the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi of the Hanafi school, does a seller's bara'at from all defects include a defect that arises after the contract but before delivery?
A) Yes, because the purpose of bara'at is to make the sale binding.
B) No, because bara'at only applies to established defects.
C) Yes, but only if the defect existed before the contract.
D) No, because bara'at is invalid for future defects.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a seller's bara'at from all defects covers both existing defects and those arising after the contract but before delivery, making the sale binding without option for the buyer.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Classification of Invalid Sales: Batil, Fasid, Mauquf, Makruh
Definition and classification of invalid sales with a refinement on batil vs. fasid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between batil and fasid sales by identifying whether the subject matter is considered property (mal) and state the legal consequences of each.

Core Idea
A sale is batil if the object is not considered property; otherwise, it is fasid, which transfers ownership upon possession.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi classification of invalid sales. The source text from al-Hidayah refines a general statement by al-Quduri, revealing a crucial distinction based on the legal status of the subject matter as property (mal). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rulings of void and voidable contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List the four types of invalid sales mentioned: batil (void), fasid (voidable), mauquf (suspended), makruh (disliked). Give a brief definition for each based on the passage.
Batil: not prescribed in essence or description. Fasid: prescribed in essence but not description. Mauquf: correct but suspended on third-party permission. Makruh: correct but with a prohibited attachment.
2
Distinguish batil from fasid using the mal criterion
Analyze the Hidayah's refinement: a sale is batil when the consideration is not considered mal (property) by anyone, e.g., carrion, blood, free person. A sale is fasid when the consideration is considered mal by some, e.g., wine or pig.
The pillar (rukn) of sale is mubadala al-mal bi al-mal. If the object is not mal, the pillar is absent.
3
Apply the classification to examples
For each example (carrion, blood, wine, pig, free person), determine whether the sale is batil or fasid based on the mal status.
Carrion and blood: batil (not mal by any). Wine and pig: fasid (mal by some). Free person: batil (not mal in property sense).
4
Contrast the legal effects
Batil: no ownership transfer even after possession. Fasid: ownership transfers upon possession but requires rescission. Mauquf: conditional transfer. Makruh: transfer with dislike.
Example: freeing a slave after possession: if sale is batil, manumission ineffective; if fasid, effective and slave's price becomes due.
5
Note the refinement of al-Quduri's statement
Al-Quduri said all sales with prohibited considerations are fasid, but al-Hidayah points out the detail: if the consideration is not mal, it is actually batil.
This refinement is crucial for correct application.

Chapter of the voidable sale (Bai' Fasid). Translation: This chapter is about the voidable sale. Jamil Ahmad, may Allah forgive him. Explanation: Having completed the discussion of the two types of valid sales—binding and non-binding—in this chapter the rulings of invalid sales are mentioned. Invalid sales are of four types: Batil (void), Mauquf (suspended), Fasid (voidable), and Makruh (disliked). Bai' Batil is that which is not prescribed (mashru') in both essence (dhat) and description (wasf), like the sale of a non-existent item or the sale of a non-property like carrion, etc. Bai' Fasid is that which is prescribed in essence but not in description, like the sale of something not capable of delivery, i.e., selling that which one cannot hand over, such as a runaway slave, or inserting a condition contrary to the contract. Makruh is that which is prescribed in both essence and description, but a prohibited action has become attached to it, like selling after the Friday adhan. Bai' Mauquf is that which is correct in both essence and description, but its execution is suspended on the permission of a third party other than the contractors, like the sale of a slave is suspended on the permission of the master. Bai' Batil is completely not beneficial for ownership, neither before possession nor after possession. For example, if someone bought a slave in exchange for carrion, took possession, and freed him, the slave would not be freed because the sale of carrion is batil. Bai' Fasid becomes beneficial for ownership upon possession. For example, if someone bought a slave in exchange for wine, took possession, and freed him, the slave would be freed from the buyer's side, and the price of the slave would become obligatory upon him because the sale in exchange for wine is fasid. Bai' Mauquf is beneficial for ownership on a suspended basis, and Bai' Makruh is beneficial for ownership with dislike. Since the valid sale is the original, it was mentioned first, and the invalid sale later. Since the fasid sale occurs frequently due to many causes, the word 'fasid' is mentioned in the title, although the rulings of batil, makruh, and mauquf are also included here. The criterion for a sale being batil or fasid: And when one or both of the considerations are prohibited, then the sale is fasid, like the sale in exchange for carrion, blood, wine, or pig. Likewise, when it is not owned, like a free person. Al-Quduri said: 'These are chapters that he collected, but there is detail in them; we will explain it, Allah willing.' So we say: The sale in exchange for carrion and blood is batil, and likewise in exchange for a free person, due to the absence of the pillar of sale, which is the exchange of property for property, because these things are not considered property by anyone. And the sale in exchange for wine and pig is fasid, because the reality of sale—exchange of property for property—exists, since wine and pig are property according to some. Translation: And when one or both considerations are prohibited, then the sale is fasid, like the sale in exchange for carrion, blood, wine, or pig, and likewise when it is not owned, like a free person. The author of Hidayah says that Quduri has collected these cases, but there is detail in them which we will explain, Allah willing. So we say: The sale in exchange for carrion or blood is batil, and likewise in exchange for a free person is batil, because the pillar of sale is absent and it is the exchange of property for property, because these things are not considered property by anyone. And the sale in exchange for wine and pig is fasid, because the reality of sale—exchange of property for property—exists, because wine and pig are property according to some. Explanation: Al-Quduri said that if the sold item or the price, or both, are prohibited by shari'ah, then the sale is fasid, like making carrion, blood, wine, pig, or a free person as the price. The author of Hidayah says that Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri has declared all these cases as fasid, whereas some are fasid and some are batil. The detail is this: If carrion, blood, or a free person is made as the price, and cloth etc. as the sold item, then in these cases the sale will be batil, because the pillar of sale is the mutual exchange of property for property, and the mentioned things like carrion are not property according to followers of a revealed religion.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification ruling from the beginning of the chapter on Bai' Fasid. It first categorizes invalid sales into four types: Batil (void), Fasid (voidable), Mauquf (suspended), and Makruh (disliked). Each is defined based on whether the sale is prescribed (mashru') in its essence (dhat) and description (wasf). Batil sales lack both, Fasid sales have essence but lack description, Makruh sales have both but are associated with a prohibited act, and Mauquf sales are correct but require third-party permission. The text then gives examples: selling a non-existent item or carrion (batil), selling something not deliverable like a runaway slave (fasid), selling after Friday adhan (makruh), a slave's sale needing master's permission (mauquf). It explains the legal effects: a batil sale never transfers ownership, even after possession; a fasid sale transfers ownership upon possession (but requires rescission); a mauquf sale transfers ownership conditionally; a makruh sale transfers ownership but is disliked. The passage then addresses a refinement by the Hidayah of Al-Quduri's general statement that all sales with prohibited considerations are fasid. The Hidayah points out that when the consideration is not considered property (mal) at all, like carrion, blood, or a free person, the sale is actually batil because the pillar of sale (exchange of property) is absent. However, when the consideration is considered property by some (like wine or pig), the sale is fasid because the reality of sale exists. This distinction is crucial: the classification depends on whether the subject matter is considered mal (property) in itself.

Key Terms
بیع باطل (bay' batil): A sale that is void in both essence and description; it never transfers ownership and its subject matter is not considered property.
بیع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is valid in essence but defective in description; it transfers ownership upon possession but must be rescinded.
مال (mal): Property that is legally recognized and has value; the presence of mal in both considerations is a pillar of the sale.
رکن بیع (rukn al-bay'): The essential pillar of a sale: the mutual exchange of property for property (mubadala al-mal bi al-mal bi al-taradi).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person sells a slave in exchange for carrion (maitah). After taking possession of the slave, the buyer frees him. What is the ruling regarding the slave's freedom according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The slave is freed because the sale is fasid and ownership transfers upon possession.
B) The slave is not freed because the sale is batil and no ownership transfers.
C) The slave is freed but the buyer must pay the seller the slave's price.
D) The sale is valid because carrion can be property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies invalid sales into four categories (batil, fasid, mauquf, makruh), and the distinction between batil and fasid hinges on whether the subject matter is recognized as property (mal).

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Classification of Sales: Sahih, Batil, Fasid, Makruh, Mawquf
Overview of five categories of sales with definitions and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the five categories of sales in Hanafi fiqh and state the legal effect of each category on ownership and liability.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a defective sale (fasid) can become valid upon possession, while a void sale (batil) cannot.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents the foundational classification of sales. Understanding the differences between sahih, batil, fasid, makruh, and mawquf is essential for determining the legal consequences of a transaction. The passage provides definitions and concrete examples to illustrate how each category affects ownership and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories and their core definitions
List the five terms: sahih, batil, fasid, makruh, mawquf. Write a one-sentence definition for each based on the passage.
Pay attention to how each category relates to the concepts of essence (dhāt) and description (waṣf).
2
Analyze the basis for each classification
Determine whether each category is valid in essence and/or description. For example, a sahih sale is valid in both; a fasid sale is valid in essence but deficient in description (e.g., unclear price).
The passage explicitly mentions these criteria for some categories.
3
Understand the examples of batil and fasid
The passage gives examples: selling a slave for carrion (batil) vs. for wine (fasid). Note why carrion is not considered mal, while wine is considered mal by some views.
The distinction hinges on whether the subject matter or price is considered property (māl) in Hanafi law.
4
Distinguish batil and fasid based on the effect of possession
Batil sales never confer ownership, even after possession. Fasid sales confer ownership upon possession, but the defective element (e.g., unlawful price) must be corrected.
This is a unique Hanafi feature; other schools like Shafi'i do not make this distinction.
5
Note the positions of makruh and mawquf
Makruh sales are valid but involve a disliked act (e.g., selling after Friday adhan). Mawquf sales are valid subject to third-party approval (e.g., a slave's sale pending master's permission).
Both are valid in essence and description but carry additional conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيوع
كتاب البيوع
۱۵۶
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ہشتم جو ذات کے اعتبار سے مشروع ہو لیکن وصف کے اعتبار سے مشروع نہ ہو جیسے غیر مقدور التسلیم کی بیع یعنی ایسی چیز کو فروخت کرنا جس کے سپرد کرنے پر قادر نہ ہو جیسا کہ بھاگا ہوا غلام یا مقتضی عقد کے خلاف شرط کرنا مکروہ ہے جو ذات اور وصف دونوں اعتبار سے مشروع ہو۔ لیکن فعل منہی عنہ اس کے متصل ہو گیا ہو جیسے اذان جمعہ کے بعد فروخت کرنا اور بیع موقوف وہ ہے جو ذات اور وصف دونوں اعتبار سے درست ہو مگر اس کا نفاذ عاقدین کے علاوہ کسی تیسرے کی اجازت پر موقوف ہو۔ جیسے غلام کی بیع مولی کی اجازت پر موقوف ہوتی ہے۔ بیع باطل بالکل مفید ملک نہیں ہوتی نہ قبضہ سے پہلے اور نہ قبضہ کے بعد ۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے مردار کے عوض غلام خریدا اور اس پر قبضہ کر کے اس کو آزاد کر دیا تو غلام آزاد نہ ہو گا۔ کیونکہ مردار کی بیع باطل ہوتی ہے اور بیع فاسد قبضہ کرنے سے مفید ملک ہوتی ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی نے شراب کے عوض غلام خریدا اور اس پر قبضہ کر کے اس کو آزاد کر دیا تو یہ غلام مشتری کی جانب سے آزاد ہو جائے گا اور اس پر غلام کی قیمت واجب ہو گی ۔ کیونکہ شراب کے عوض بیع فاسد ہوتی ہے اور بیع موقوف علی سبیل التوقف مفید ملک ہوتی ہے۔ اور بیع مکروہ کراہت کے ساتھ مفید ملک ہوتی ہے۔
Book of Sales. Ashraf al-Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah, volume 8. A sale that is valid in essence but not valid in description, like a sale of something not deliverable, i.e., selling something one cannot hand over, like an escaped slave. Or a condition contrary to the requirement of the contract is makruh. A sale that is valid in both essence and description but a prohibited act has become attached to it, like selling after the Friday adhan. And a mawquf sale is one that is correct in both essence and description but its execution depends on the permission of a third party besides the contracting parties, like a slave's sale depends on the master's permission. A batil sale does not confer ownership at all, neither before possession nor after. For example, if someone buys a slave in exchange for carrion and takes possession and frees him, the slave is not free because the sale of carrion is batil. A fasid sale confers ownership upon possession. For example, if someone buys a slave in exchange for wine and takes possession and frees him, that slave becomes free from the buyer's side, and the price of the slave becomes obligatory upon him because the sale with wine is fasid. A mawquf sale confers ownership on the basis of suspension. A makruh sale confers ownership with dislike.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage from the commentary defines the five categories of sales in Hanafi fiqh: sahih (valid), batil (void), fasid (defective), makruh (disliked), and mawquf (suspended). The explanation highlights that the ruling depends on the nature of the subject matter (mabi') and the price (thaman). A sale is valid when both are permissible (mubah) and the contract meets all conditions. Batil sales are entirely void and do not transfer ownership at all, even with possession. Fasid sales become valid upon possession but require correction of the defect. Makruh sales are valid but disliked. Mawquf sales are valid subject to approval. The examples illustrate the distinction: selling a slave for carrion is batil (carrion is not mal), so freeing the slave does not make him free. Selling a slave for wine is fasid (wine is considered mal by some), so freeing the slave upon possession is effective and the buyer owes the slave's value. The passage follows the order of the original matn, first explaining the definitions then giving practical examples.

Key Terms
بیع صحیح (bay' sahih): A valid sale that is lawful in its essence (dhāt) and description (waṣf) and fulfills all conditions of the contract.
بیع باطل (bay' bāṭil): A void sale that lacks an essential element (rukn), such as exchange of mal for mal, and thus does not confer ownership.
بیع فاسد (bay' fāsid): A defective sale that is valid in essence but has a legal defect (e.g., unclear price) which can be rectified; becomes valid upon possession.
بیع مکروہ (bay' makruh): A disliked sale that is valid in both essence and description but involves a prohibited act (e.g., selling after Friday adhan).
بیع موقوف (bay' mawquf): A suspended sale that is correct in essence and description but depends on a third party's permission for execution (e.g., a slave's sale).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if someone buys a slave in exchange for wine, takes possession, and frees the slave?
A) The slave is not freed because the sale is batil.
B) The slave is freed and the buyer owes the slave's value.
C) The sale is valid and the slave is freed without any further obligation.
D) The slave is freed only if the seller permits.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely distinguishes between batil (void) and fasid (defective) sales, a distinction with significant legal consequences for ownership and liability.

Batil vs Fasid: Distinguishing Void and Defective Sales with Unlawful Prices
Ruling with proof distinguishing batil and fasid based on the legal status of the price as mal or non-mal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a sale with a haram price is batil or fasid based on whether the price is considered mal (wealth) and deliverable.

Core Idea
If the price is not mal (carrion, blood, free person), the sale is batil; if it is mal but undeliverable (wine for a Muslim), the sale is fasid.

This passage corrects a common simplification by Imam al-Quduri, who labelled all unlawful-price sales as fasid. The Hidayah author provides a crucial refinement: some prices lack the quality of mal entirely, making the sale batil (void), while others are considered mal by non-Muslims but cannot be legally delivered by a Muslim, resulting in a fasid (defective) sale. Understanding this distinction is essential for determining ownership and liability in such transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item used as price
Look at the thing given in exchange for the subject matter. The passage discusses carrion, blood, free person, wine, and pig.
2
Determine if the item is mal (wealth) according to Hanafi definition
Use the definition: something towards which human nature inclines and can be stored for need. Carrion, blood, and free persons are not mal; wine and pig are mal for ahl al-dhimma.
Mal is defined by customary usage, not just ownership.
3
Classify the sale as batil or fasid
If the price is not mal, the sale is batil because the pillar of exchanging mal for mal is absent. If the price is mal but not deliverable by the seller (e.g., a Muslim cannot legally deliver wine), the sale is fasid.
Batil: no ownership transfer; Fasid: ownership transfers upon possession.
4
Verify the ruling with the text's reasoning
Read the passage: for carrion/blood/free person, it says 'sale is batil' because these are not mal; for wine/pig, it says 'sale is fasid' because they are mal for some but the seller cannot deliver.
The author explicitly refutes the general statement of al-Quduri.
5
Note the practical effect
In a batil sale, the buyer never becomes owner; in a fasid sale, the buyer becomes owner upon taking possession (qabd) and the sale can be validated if the defect is removed.
This has consequences for liability if the item perishes.

And when one of the two exchanges or both are unlawful, then the sale is fasid, like a sale for carrion, blood, wine, and pig, and likewise if it is not owned, like a free person. The author of al-Hidayah says: Al-Quduri collected these cases, but there is detail in them which we will explain, Allah willing. So we say: The sale for carrion and blood is batil, and likewise for a free person, due to the absence of the pillar of sale, which is the exchange of mal for mal, for these things are not considered mal by anyone. And the sale for wine and pig is fasid because the reality of sale, i.e., exchange of mal for mal, exists, for they are mal according to some. Explanation: Al-Quduri said that if the subject matter or the price or both are unlawful, then the sale is fasid, like making carrion, blood, wine, pig, or a free person the price. The author of al-Hidayah says: Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Quduri considered all these cases as fasid, whereas some are fasid and some are batil. The detail is: If carrion, blood, or a free person is made the price, and cloth etc. is made the subject matter, then in these cases the sale is batil because the pillar of sale is the exchange of mal for mal with mutual consent, and these things are not considered mal by anyone among the followers of any revealed religion. Because mal is that towards which human nature inclines and which can be stored for times of need. Since there is no natural inclination towards carrion and blood nor are they stored, and similarly a free person is not stored, these things are not mal. And since they are not mal, the exchange of mal for mal does not occur; rather, the exchange of mal for non-mal occurs, while the pillar of sale is the exchange of mal for mal. Thus the pillar is absent, and when the pillar is absent, the thing becomes batil. Therefore in these cases the sale is batil. And if wine or pig is made the price, then the sale is fasid because wine and pig are considered mal by the People of the Covenant (ahl al-dhimma). Thus the reality of sale is found due to the exchange of mal for mal. But since a Muslim is not able to deliver nor take possession of wine and pig, it becomes fasid due to the price being something not deliverable. Does a batil sale confer ownership? And the batil does not confer ownership of disposal; and if the bought item perishes in the buyer's hand, it is considered a trust according to some scholars because the contract is not valid, so the possession remains by the permission of the owner. According to others, it is guaranteed (daman) because it is not in a lower state than that taken for consideration of purchase. It is said that the first is the saying of Abu Hanifa and the second is the saying of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), as in the sale of umm al-walad and mudabbar, as we will explain, Allah willing. And the fasid sale confers ownership upon possession, and the sold item is guaranteed in the buyer's hand. There is a difference with Imam al-Shafi'i, and we will explain this later, Allah willing. And similarly, the sale of carrion, blood, and a free person is batil because they are not mal, so they cannot be the subject of sale. Difference between batil and fasid. Explanation: In this passage, the difference between batil and fasid is explained. It is stated that the batil sale does not confer ownership of disposal nor ownership of the essence. So in a batil sale, the buyer does not own the specific sold item nor does he have ownership to dispose of it. And in the batil sale, if the sold item perishes in the buyer's possession, according to some scholars it is a trust, i.e., no guarantee is incumbent on the buyer, neither the price nor its value is due, because the contract is batil and a batil contract is not valid, and the buyer's possession is by the permission of the owner. Therefore...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed ruling on sales where the price (thaman) is unlawful. The author, citing the matn of al-Hidayah, first notes that Imam al-Quduri lumped all such cases together as fasid. However, the author corrects this by distinguishing: if the price is carrion, blood, or a free person, the sale is batil (void) because these are not considered mal (wealth) in any legal system. The definition of mal is given: something towards which human nature inclines and can be stored for need. Carrion, blood, and a free person do not meet this definition. Since the sale's pillar is the exchange of mal for mal, the absence of mal on one side makes the pillar absent, rendering the sale batil. In contrast, wine and pig are considered mal by non-Muslims (ahl al-dhimma), so the exchange of mal for mal exists, but because a Muslim cannot legally deliver or receive these items, the sale is fasid (defective) due to inability to deliver. The passage then clarifies the legal effects: a batil sale transfers no ownership at all, and if the purchased item is destroyed in the buyer's possession, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa holds it is a trust (no liability), while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is guaranteed (buyer must pay the price or value). This dispute is analogous to the sale of umm al-walad and mudabbar. In contrast, a fasid sale transfers ownership upon possession, and the buyer is liable for the item. The passage ends by reiterating that batil sales are those involving items that are not mal at all. The commentary follows the matn closely, explaining each point with examples.

Key Terms
مال (māl): Wealth: something towards which human nature inclines and which can be stored for times of need. Carrion, blood, and free persons are not mal; wine and pig are mal for non-Muslims.
بیع باطل (bay' bāṭil): A void sale lacking the pillar of exchange of mal for mal; does not confer ownership and buyer is not liable for destruction (according to Abu Hanifa) or is liable (according to Sahibayn).
بیع فاسد (bay' fāsid): A defective sale that is valid in essence (exchange of mal for mal) but has a defect that can be corrected, such as inability to deliver the price; upon possession, it becomes valid and the buyer is liable.
ثمن (thaman): The price given in exchange for the subject matter in a sale. In this passage, the thaman may be unlawful (haram) or non-mal.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee; in a batil sale, the buyer may be liable for the price or value of the destroyed item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a sale made with wine as the price considered fasid rather than batil in Hanafi law?
A) Because wine is not considered mal by anyone.
B) Because wine is considered mal by some people (ahl al-dhimma), so the exchange of mal for mal exists.
C) Because the seller can legally deliver wine.
D) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited wine but not in sales.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes batil (void) from fasid (defective) based on whether the price is mal and deliverable, providing a nuanced approach to sales with unlawful prices.

Liability in a Batil Sale: The Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability (daman) when the sold item perishes in a batil sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on liability when the sold item perishes in a batil sale and identify the practical consequence of each.

Core Idea
In a batil sale, Abu Hanifa says the buyer is not liable (trust), but the Sahibayn say the buyer is liable (guaranteed) – a dispute with practical impact on risk allocation.

After establishing the distinction between batil and fasid, the passage addresses a critical question: if a sold item perishes in the buyer's possession in a batil sale, who bears the loss? Two views exist within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa considers the possession as a trust (amanah) with no liability, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the buyer is liable (daman). This dispute echoes analogous cases in the sale of umm al-walad and mudabbar, and later Hanafi works often prefer the view of the Sahibayn for commercial stability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario: batil sale with perish of sold item
Focus on a sale that has been ruled batil (e.g., price is carrion) and the item (e.g., cloth) is destroyed in the buyer's hands. The question is whether the buyer must compensate the seller.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa says the buyer is not liable; the item is a trust (amanah) because the contract is void and possession was with the owner's permission.
The reasoning: the batil contract is not given effect, so possession remains as an amanah from the owner.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the buyer is liable; the possession is guaranteed (mamun) because it is analogous to possession taken during purchase negotiations, which is at least that strong.
The reasoning: The buyer's possession is not inferior to possession taken for 'consideration of purchase' (soom al-shira'), which is always guaranteed.
4
Recognize the dispute's source
The text notes the same disagreement occurs in sales of umm al-walad and mudabbar, which are also batil. This shows the dispute is a recurring theme in Hanafi fiqh.
The text promises to explain those cases later (in another passage).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical effect: If the item is destroyed, under Abu Hanifa the seller loses the value; under the Sahibayn, the buyer must pay the value or price.
Which view is chosen affects who bears the risk in invalid transactions.

Since there is no natural inclination towards carrion and blood nor are they stored, and similarly a free person is not stored, these things are not mal. Thus the pillar is absent, and when the pillar is absent, the thing becomes batil. Therefore in these cases the sale is batil. Thus the reality of sale is found due to the exchange of mal for mal. Commentary
This passage presents a detailed ruling on sales where the price (thaman) is unlawful. The author, citing the matn of al-Hidayah, first notes that Imam al-Quduri lumped all such cases together as fasid. However, the author corrects this by distinguishing: if the price is carrion, blood, or a free person, the sale is batil (void) because these are not considered mal (wealth) in any legal system. The definition of mal is given: something towards which human nature inclines and can be stored for need. Carrion, blood, and a free person do not meet this definition. Since the sale's pillar is the exchange of mal for mal, the absence of mal on one side makes the pillar absent, rendering the sale batil. In contrast, wine and pig are considered mal by non-Muslims (ahl al-dhimma), so the exchange of mal for mal exists, but because a Muslim cannot legally deliver or receive these items, the sale is fasid (defective) due to inability to deliver. The passage then clarifies the legal effects: a batil sale transfers no ownership at all, and if the purchased item is destroyed in the buyer's possession, there is a dispute: Abu Hanifa holds it is a trust (no liability), while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is guaranteed (buyer must pay the price or value). This dispute is analogous to the sale of umm al-walad and mudabbar. In contrast, a fasid sale transfers ownership upon possession, and the buyer is liable for the item. The passage ends by reiterating that batil sales are those involving items that are not mal at all. The commentary follows the matn closely, explaining each point with examples.

Key Terms
امانت (amānat): Trust: possession without liability for loss unless negligence is proven. In batil sale, Abu Hanifa considers the buyer's possession as a trust.
مضمون (mamūn): Guaranteed: possession that carries liability, meaning the possessor must compensate if the item is destroyed.
سوم شراء (soom al-shira): Consideration of purchase: a situation where a potential buyer takes possession of an item to examine it with the intention of possibly buying it. Such possession is guaranteed.
ام ولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who gives birth to her master's child. Her sale is batil, and the liability for her destruction is disputed similarly.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who is promised freedom upon the master's death. Sale of mudabbar is batil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the Sahibayn's view that the buyer is liable in a batil sale?
A) They consider the sale valid despite its batil nature.
B) They analogize the buyer's possession to possession taken for consideration of purchase (soom al-shira).
C) They rely on a hadith stating 'possession carries liability'.
D) They hold the seller legally responsible for the transaction.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on liability in batil sales: Abu Hanifa sees no liability (trust), while the Sahibayn impose liability (guaranteed), with later preference often for the latter.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Void (Batil) Sales and Liability for Perished Goods
Defines batil sale and discusses liability disagreement among Hanafi imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of a batil sale and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when the sold item perishes.

Core Idea
A void sale confers no ownership; liability for perished goods is disputed: Abu Hanifa says amanah, Sahibayn say daman.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies the nature of a void (batil) sale and addresses a key disagreement within the Hanafi school. Understanding this dispute—whether the buyer is liable if the item is destroyed—requires grasping the underlying analogy to goods taken on approval and the definition of property (mal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on batil sale
First, note that a batil sale does not confer ownership of disposition (tasarruf) or of the specific item (raqabah). The contract is void ab initio.
2
Understand the dispute on liability
After stating the basic ruling, the text presents a disagreement: some scholars say the buyer holds the item as amanah (no liability), others say it is daman (liable). The first view is attributed to Abu Hanifa, the second to the Sahibayn.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the analogy to maqbud 'ala sam al-shira'
The proponents of daman analogize the batil sale to goods taken on approval (maqbud 'ala sam al-shira'), where liability is established. They argue that a batil sale is stronger in establishing liability because there is an actual intent to purchase.
This is a qiyas based on the 'illa of potential ownership.
4
Apply the definition of mal to examples
The text lists carrion, blood, and free persons as examples of items that are not mal, hence any sale involving them as subject matter or price is batil. This illustrates the fundamental condition that the subject matter must be property.
This clarifies the boundary of valid sale.

Source Text
